Dyeing Stolen Property: Rulings on Return and Compensation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dye stolen goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on dyeing a stolen garment before amputation and explain the differences among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Red dye: thief keeps; black dye: thief returns. Custom changes the ruling.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the consequences of changing the form of stolen property. At issue is whether dyeing a stolen white cloth red or black before the hadd amputation affects the obligation to return the cloth or pay compensation. The answer depends on whether the dye is considered an increase (red) or a decrease (black), and whether the thief's ownership of the new object extinguishes the owner's rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and timing
Confirm that the dyeing occurs before amputation (the text explicitly conditions this). Note the object: a stolen white cloth, then dyed either red or black.
Timing matters: dyeing after amputation leads to a different ruling (return required).
2
State the ruling for red dye
According to the majority (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), if the thief dyes the cloth red before amputation, he is amputated for the original theft, but he does not return the dyed cloth nor compensate the owner if it is destroyed. This covers three sub-rulings: amputation is obligatory, no return, no guarantee.
Imam Muhammad has a different view: return the cloth and pay for the dye increase, by analogy with usurpation.
3
Contrast with black dye
For black dye, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the thief must return the cloth, because blackness is considered a decrease (people did not wear black at that time). Abu Yusuf disagrees, treating black like red.
This disagreement is based on 'urf (custom) and not on a fundamental legal principle.
4
Examine the proofs and tarjih
The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) use qiyas with grinding wheat: the thief owns the flour but is cut for the wheat. For red dye, the thief owns the dyed cloth but is cut for the white. The owner's right in the cloth is only formal (exists in form but not in meaning, since the cloth as white is not guaranteed). In contrast, in usurpation both parties have full rights. The mufta bihi is the view of the two.
Imam Muhammad's analogy with usurpation is rejected because the owner's right is weaker here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخرجها لا ولو صنع المسروق دراهم أو دنانير قطع وردها ولو صبغه أحمر فقطع لا يرد ولا يضمن ولو اسود يرده . عندهما خلافاً له. ثم وجوب القطع لا يشكل على قوله لأنه لم يملكه، وقيل على قولهما لا يجب لأنه ملكه قبل القطع، وقيل يجب لأنه صار بالصنعة شيئاً آخر فلم يملك عينه . وأشار إلى أنه لو صنع المسروق من النقد آنية كان كذلك بالأولى. وقيد بالنقد لأنه في الحديد والرصاص والصفر إن جعله أواني فإن كان يباع عدداً فهو للسارق بالإجماع وإن كان يباع وزناً فهو على الاختلاف بينهم في الذهب والفضة؛ كذا في شرح المختار. وذكر الأسبيجابي أنه لو سرق حنطة فطحنها تكون للسارق بعد القطع . قوله : (ولو صبغه أحمر فقطع لا يرد ولا يضمن بيان لثلاثة أحكام الأول وجوب القطع لأن قطع السارق باعتبار سرقة الثوب الأبيض وهو لم يملكه أبيض بوجه ما والمملوك للسارق إنما هو المصبوغ فصار كما إذا سرق حنطة فطحنها فإنه يقطع بالحنطة وإن ملك الدقيق الثاني عدم رده إلى المسروق منه وهو قولهما. وقال محمد : يؤخذ منه الثوب ويعطى ما زاد الصبغ فيه اعتباراً للغصب، والجامع كون الثوب أصلاً قائماً وكون الصبغ تابعاً ولهما أن الصبغ قائم صورة ومعنى حتى لو أراد مصبوغاً يضمن ما زاد الصبغ فيه، وحق المالك في الثوب قائم صورة لا معنى أنه غير مضمون على السارق بالهلاك وهو الحكم الثالث الذي أفاده بقوله «ولا يضمن» أي لا يرده حال قيامه ولا يضمنه حال استهلاكه بخلاف الغصب لأن حق كل واحد قائم صورة ومعنى فاستويا من هذا الوجه ، ورجحنا جانب المالك لما ذكرنا قيد بكونه صبغه قبل القطع بدليل فاء التعقيب لأنه لو صبغه بعد القطع يرده لأن الشركة بعد القطع لا تسقط القطع؛ كذا في شرح المختار . وذكر في الهداية الصبغ بعد القطع فإنه قال : وإن سرق ثوباً فقطع فصبغه أحمر لم يؤخذ منه الثوب ولا يضمن اهـ. وهو مفيد لأنه لو صبغه قبل القطع فالحكم كذلك بالأولى وكلام محمد دليل عليه أيضاً فإنه قال : سرق الثوب فقطع يده وقد صبغ الثوب أحمر لم يؤخذ منه الثوب قوله : ولو اسود يرد أي لو صبغة السارق أسود يرده على المالك أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند أبي يوسف هذا والأول سواء لأن السواد عنده زيادة كالحمرة وعند محمد زيادة أيضاً كالحمرة لكنه لا يقطع حق المالك لما مر ، وعند أبي حنيفة السواد نقصان فلا يوجب انقطاع حق المالك. قالوا وهذا اختلاف عصر وزمان لا حجة وبرهان فإن الناس كانوا لا يلبسون السواد في زمنه ويلبسونه في زمنهما. وفي شرح الطحاوي: لو سرق سويقاً بسمن أو عسل فهو مثل الاختلاف في الصبغ الأحمر والله أعلم.
And he brings it out? No? If he made the stolen item into dirhams or dinars, cutting applies and he returns it. If he dyed it red and then cut, he does not return nor does he guarantee. If he dyed it black, he returns it. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to him (Abu Hanifa). Then the obligation of cutting is not problematic according to his (Abu Hanifa's) view because he did not own it. It is said according to their view it is not obligatory because he owned it before cutting, and it is said it is obligatory because it became something else by the craftsmanship so he did not own the same object. And he indicated that if he made the stolen coinage into vessels, it is likewise a fortiori. And it is restricted to coinage because in the case of iron, lead, and brass, if he made them into vessels, if they are sold by count, it belongs to the thief by consensus; if sold by weight, it is according to the difference among them regarding gold and silver. Thus in Sharh al-Mukhtar. And al-Asbijabi mentioned that if he stole wheat and ground it, it belongs to the thief after the amputation. His statement: (And if he dyed it red and then cut, he does not return nor guarantee) is an explanation of three rulings: first, the obligation of amputation because the amputation of the thief is on account of the theft of the white cloth, and he did not own it white at all; what is owned by the thief is only the dyed cloth, so it becomes like when he stole wheat and ground it: he is amputated on account of the wheat even though he owns the flour. Second, the non‑return to the victim, which is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Muhammad said: the garment is taken from him and he is given the increase of the dye, considering usurpation (ghasb); the common factor is that the garment remains as an original and the dye is subsidiary. According to them, the dye is existent both in form and meaning, so much so that if one wanted dyed cloth, he would guarantee the increase of the dye. The right of the owner in the garment exists in form only, not in meaning, because it is not guaranteed upon the thief by destruction. This is the third ruling which he conveyed by his statement 'nor does he guarantee', meaning he neither returns it while it exists nor guarantees it when it is destroyed, unlike usurpation because the right of each is existent in form and meaning, so they are equal in this respect, and we have given preponderance to the side of the owner for what we mentioned. It is conditioned that he dyed it before the amputation by the evidence of the letter fa indicating sequence; because if he dyed it after amputation, he would return it because partnership after amputation does not cancel the amputation. Thus in Sharh al-Mukhtar. And it is mentioned in al-Hidaya regarding dyeing after amputation: he said: If he stole a garment and was amputated, then dyed it red, the garment is not taken from him nor does he guarantee. It is useful because if he dyed it before amputation, the ruling is likewise a fortiori. And the statement of Muhammad is also evidence for it, for he said: He stole the garment and his hand was cut while he had dyed the garment red, the garment is not taken from him. His statement: 'And if black, he returns it' means if the thief dyed it black, he returns it to the owner according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, this and the first are the same because blackness is an increase like redness. According to Muhammad, it is also an increase like redness but it does not cut off the owner's right due to what has passed. According to Abu Hanifa, blackness is a decrease, so it does not cause the termination of the owner's right. They said: This is a difference of era and time, not a proof and evidence, because people did not wear black in his time and wore it in their time. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If he stole sawiq with butter or honey, it is like the difference regarding red dye. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of changing the form of stolen property (specifically dyeing a garment) on the hadd punishment (amputation) and the return/compensation of the item. The ruling: if a person steals a white cloth and dyes it red before being caught and amputated, he is still amputated (because the theft was of the white cloth, which he never owned); however, he does not return the dyed cloth and does not guarantee its value if destroyed. This is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two). Imam Muhammad adds an opinion: he would take the cloth and give the owner the increase due to the dye, by analogy with usurpation (ghasb). But the relied-upon view is that of the two. If the dye is black, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the thief must return the cloth because blackness is considered a decrease (people did not wear black at that time), so the cloth is still substantially the owner's property. Abu Yusuf disagrees, considering black also an increase. The passage also covers analogous cases: making stolen coinage into vessels (rules depend on whether sold by count or weight), grinding wheat, and mixing sawiq with butter/honey. The key principle is whether the change creates a new object (milk) or merely adds value. The timing of dyeing (before or after amputation) is critical: if after, the thief must return it because partnership after amputation does not affect the hadd.

Key Terms
صبغ (sabgh): Dyeing a stolen garment, which changes its form and creates a dispute about ownership.
قطع (qat'): Amputation of the hand as hadd punishment for theft.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; illegal possession of another's property with the object itself remaining.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a thief steals a white cloth and dyes it red before being caught and amputated, what is the Hanafi ruling (according to the two, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding returning the cloth?
A) He must return the cloth and is compensated for the dye.
B) He does not return the cloth and does not compensate.
C) He returns the cloth and also compensates.
D) He is not amputated because he now owns the dyed cloth.

Key Takeaway
For red dye before amputation, the relied-upon Hanafi position is that the thief keeps the garment without compensation; for black dye, custom may require return.

Betrayal by the King: Fighting Without Renunciation
Ruling on fighting without renunciation due to king's betrayal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a truce is broken by the enemy's betrayal and state that fighting may commence without renunciation.

Core Idea
Betrayal by the king voids the truce for all; unauthorized betrayal affects only the traitors.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq explains a critical rule of Islamic international law: when the enemy king betrays a truce, the treaty is automatically void, and Muslims may fight without prior renunciation. We will examine the distinction between authorized and unauthorized betrayal and the exception for powerless individuals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the betrayal
Recognize that the king's betrayal, whether by his own action or by a group with his permission, constitutes a breach of the covenant.
The betrayal must be by the king or by a group acting with his explicit or implicit permission.
2
Determine if renunciation is needed
Since the covenant is already broken by the betrayal, no formal renunciation (nabdh) is required. Fighting can commence immediately.
This is based on the principle that renunciation is only needed for a valid covenant; a broken one needs no notice.
3
Distinguish between authorized and unauthorized betrayal
If a group enters Muslim territory without the king's permission, only those individuals are considered traitors; the truce with the rest remains intact.
The ruling applies to the entire enemy nation only if the betrayal is by the king or with his consent.
4
Note the exception for powerless traitors
If the traitorous individuals have no power (man'ah), their act does not break the truce even for them; they are simply punished individually.
Power (man'ah) means they have the capacity to cause harm or sustain a military force.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, this allows targeted action against the traitors without ending the overall truce with the enemy nation.
Muslims may kill or enslave the traitors, but the truce continues for the rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ونقاتل بلا نبذ لو خان ملكهم لأنهم صاروا ناقضين للعهد فلا حاجة إلى نقضه. أطلق في خيانة ملكهم فشمل ما إذا كان باتفاق الكل أو بفعل بعضهم بإذنه حتى لو دخل جماعة منهم ذو منعة دار الإسلام بإذنه وقاتلوا المسلمين كان نقضاً. وقيد بملكهم لأنه لو دخل جماعة بغير إذنه لم ينتقض في حق الكل، وإنما ينتقض في حق الخائنين حتى يجوز قتلهم واسترقاقهم وإن لم يكن لهم منعة لم يكن نقضاً للعهد قوله : ( والمرتدين بلا مال وإن أخذ لم يرد) أي نصالح المرتدين حتى ننظر في أمورهم لأن الإسلام مرجو منهم فجاز تأخير قتالهم طمعاً في إسلامهم، ولا نأخذ عليه مالاً لأنه لا يجوز أخذ الجزية منهم وإن أخذه لم يرده لأنه مال غير معصوم. وأشار إلى أنه يجوز الصلح مع أهل البغي بالأولى ولا يؤخذ منهم شيء، وصرح الشارح بأن أموالهم معصومة فظاهره أنه إذا أخذ شيء لأجل الصلح يرد عليهم. وفي فتح القدير: ويرد عليهم بعد ما وضعت الحرب أوزارها ولا يردها حال الحرب لأنه إعانة لهم .اهـ. وأطلق في جواز صلح المرتدين وهو مقيد بما إذا غلبوا على بلدة وصار دارهم دار الحرب وإلا فلا، لأن فيه تقرير المرتد على الردة وذلك لا يجوز ولذا قيده الفقيه أبو الليث بما ذكرنا ؛ كذا في الفتح قوله : (ولم نبع سلاحاً منهم لأن النبي عليه السلام نهي عن بيع السلاح من أهل الحرب وحمله إليهم ولأن فيه تقويتهم على قتال المسلمين فيمنع من ذلك ، وصرح الشارحون بحرمته. أراد من السلاح ما يكون سبباً لتقويتهم على الحرب فدخل الكراع والحديد لأنه أصل السلاح وهو ظاهر الرواية . والكراع الخيل . ودخل الرقيق لأنهم يتوالدون عندهم فيعودون حرباً علينا مسلماً كان الرقيق أو كافراً. وخرج الطعام والقماش والقياس المنع إلا أنا عرفناه بالنص لأنه عليه السلام أمر ثمامة يمير أهل مكة وهم حرب عليه . وشمل كلامه ما قبل الموادعة وما بعدها لأنها على شرف الانقضاء أو النقض قال الفقيه أبو الليث : وليس هذا كما قالوا في بيع العصير ممن يجعله خمراً لأن العصير ليس بآلة للمعصية وإنما : خمراً، وأما هنا فالسلاح آلة للفتنة آلة لها بعدما يصير يصير في الحال اهـ. وفي كافي الحكم فإن كان الحربي جاء بسيف فاشترى مكانه قوساً أو رمحاً أو فرساً لم يترك أن يخرج به مكان سيفه، وكذا إذا استبدل بسيفه سيفاً خيراً منه، وإن كان مثله أو اهـ. فما يمنع المسلم منه المستأمن منهم أن يدخل به دارهم وإن خرج هو بشيء أن شراً منه لم يمنع ما ذكرنا فلا يمنع من الرجوع به إلا إذا أسلم العبد. هم بدؤوا بالغدر قبل مضي المدة فقاتلهم ولم ينبذ إليهم بل سأل الله تعالى أن يعمي عليهم حتى يبغتهم، وهذا هو المذكور لجميع أهل السير والمغازي ومن تلقى القصة وذكروها .
His saying: 'And we fight without renunciation if their king betrays, because they have become violators of the covenant, so there is no need to renounce it.' He made it absolute regarding the king's betrayal, so it includes whether it is by agreement of all or by the act of some with his permission, such that if a group of them with power enters the abode of Islam with his permission and fights the Muslims, it is a breach. And he restricted it to their king, because if a group enters without his permission, it is not breached with respect to all, but only breached with respect to the traitors, so that it is permissible to kill them and enslave them. And if they have no power, it is not a breach of the covenant. His saying: 'And the apostates without wealth, and if taken it is not returned' — i.e., we make peace with the apostates so that we may look into their affairs, because Islam is hoped from them, so it is permissible to delay fighting them in hope of their Islam. We do not take wealth for it because it is not permissible to take jizya from them, and if he took it, he does not return it because it is wealth without sanctity. And he indicated that making peace with the people of rebellion (ahl al-baghy) is all the more permissible, and nothing is taken from them. The commentator explicitly stated that their wealth is inviolable, so apparently if something is taken for the sake of peace, it is returned to them. In Fath al-Qadir: It is returned to them after the war lays down its burdens, and is not returned during war because it is assistance to them. And he spoke absolutely about the permissibility of making peace with apostates, but it is restricted to when they have overcome a town and their land has become dar al-harb; otherwise not, because it would be establishing the apostate upon apostasy, which is not permissible. Hence al-Faqih Abu al-Layth restricted it as we mentioned; thus in al-Fath. His saying: 'And we do not sell weapons to them because the Prophet ﷺ prohibited selling weapons to the people of war and carrying them to them, and because it strengthens them against the Muslims, so it is prevented. And the commentators stated that it is haram. He meant by weapons that which is a cause of strengthening them for war, so horses (al-kurā') and iron are included because they are the basis of weapons, and this is the apparent transmission. And slaves are included because they procreate among them and become a war against us, whether the slave is Muslim or disbeliever. Food and cloth are excluded, and analogically they would be prohibited except that we know it by the text, because the Prophet ﷺ commanded Thumama to supply food to the people of Makkah while they were at war with him. And his speech includes what is before the truce and after it, because it is on the verge of expiry or breach. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This is not like what they said about selling grape juice to one who turns it into wine, because grape juice is not a tool for sin, but here weapons are a tool for fitna [sedition] after it becomes [a weapon]. In Kafi al-Hakim: If the harbi came with a sword and bought a bow or spear or horse in its place, he is not left to take it out in place of his sword, and similarly if he exchanged his sword for a better sword, but if it is similar or worse, he is not prevented. So what prevents the Muslim from the musta'min allowing him to bring it into their land, and if he goes out with something [worse?] it does not prevent, he is not prevented from returning with it unless the slave becomes Muslim. They initiated treachery before the expiry of the period, so he fought them and did not renounce to them, but rather asked Allah to blind them so that he could take them by surprise, and this is what is mentioned by all the historians of expeditions and campaigns and those who related the story.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct rulings from the matn. First, 'we fight without renunciation if their king betrays' — because the king's betrayal itself breaks the covenant, making renunciation unnecessary. The betrayal includes an act by a group with the king's permission, but if a group acts without his permission, only those individuals are considered traitors; the truce with the rest remains intact. Second, 'the apostates without wealth' — a peace deal with apostates is allowed but only if they have seized a territory and become dominant; otherwise, it is forbidden as it would condone apostasy. No jizya can be taken from them, and wealth taken by mistake is not returned because it lacks sanctity. The commentator notes that a peace with rebels (ahl al-baghy) is even more permissible and their wealth is inviolable, so it must be returned after war. Third, 'we do not sell weapons to them' — this is haram. Weapons include anything that strengthens them militarily, such as horses, iron, and slaves (who may fight or produce offspring). Food and cloth are excluded due to the Prophet's command to Thumama. The prohibition applies both before and after a truce. The passage ends with a historical note: the Prophet fought the polytheists of Makkah without renunciation because they had already broken the treaty.

Key Terms
نبذ (nabdh): Renunciation; formal notice of termination of a treaty before hostilities begin.
خيانة (khiyanah): Betrayal; specifically the king's breach of the treaty.
منعة (man'ah): Power or strength; the capacity to resist or wage war.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the king's betrayal break the truce for the entire enemy nation?
A) Only if the king personally attacks.
B) If the king betrays or authorizes a group with power.
C) If any citizen attacks.
D) Never; renunciation is always required.

Key Takeaway
The king's betrayal, whether direct or authorized, voids the truce for all, but unauthorized betrayal only affects the individuals, provided they have power.

Peace with Apostates: Conditions and Restrictions
Ruling on peace with apostates with restrictions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on making peace with apostates and identify the condition that such peace is only permissible when apostates have seized territory and become a separate dar al-harb.

Core Idea
Peace with apostates is allowed only if they have seized territory; otherwise, it confirms apostasy.

This passage addresses a specific situation: can Muslims make a temporary peace with apostates (murtaddin) who have gathered strength? The Hanafi school permits it under strict conditions, primarily to encourage their return to Islam. We will examine the restriction imposed by Abu al-Layth and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling on apostates
Apostates (murtaddin) are normally to be fought unless they repent. However, if they have become a dominant force in a territory, a truce may be considered.
The default is fighting, but a delay is allowed in hope of their return to Islam.
2
Understand the ruling on taking wealth
No jizya can be taken from apostates; if wealth is mistakenly taken, it is not returned because it lacks sanctity (ismah).
This differs from rebels (ahl al-baghy), whose wealth is inviolable.
3
Identify the condition for a valid peace
The peace is only valid if the apostates have overcome a town and their territory has become dar al-harb. Otherwise, it would be establishing them on apostasy, which is forbidden.
This restriction is attributed to al-Faqih Abu al-Layth and is mentioned in Fath al-Qadir.
4
Compare with rebels (ahl al-baghy)
Making peace with rebels is even more permissible, and their wealth is considered inviolable, so it must be returned after war.
The Hanafi school makes a distinction: rebels are still Muslims, apostates are not, hence different treatment.

Source Text

قوله : (ونقاتل بلا نبذ لو خان ملكهم لأنهم صاروا ناقضين للعهد فلا حاجة إلى نقضه. أطلق في خيانة ملكهم فشمل ما إذا كان باتفاق الكل أو بفعل بعضهم بإذنه حتى لو دخل جماعة منهم ذو منعة دار الإسلام بإذنه وقاتلوا المسلمين كان نقضاً. وقيد بملكهم لأنه لو دخل جماعة بغير إذنه لم ينتقض في حق الكل، وإنما ينتقض في حق الخائنين حتى يجوز قتلهم واسترقاقهم وإن لم يكن لهم منعة لم يكن نقضاً للعهد قوله : ( والمرتدين بلا مال وإن أخذ لم يرد) أي نصالح المرتدين حتى ننظر في أمورهم لأن الإسلام مرجو منهم فجاز تأخير قتالهم طمعاً في إسلامهم، ولا نأخذ عليه مالاً لأنه لا يجوز أخذ الجزية منهم وإن أخذه لم يرده لأنه مال غير معصوم. وأشار إلى أنه يجوز الصلح مع أهل البغي بالأولى ولا يؤخذ منهم شيء، وصرح الشارح بأن أموالهم معصومة فظاهره أنه إذا أخذ شيء لأجل الصلح يرد عليهم. وفي فتح القدير: ويرد عليهم بعد ما وضعت الحرب أوزارها ولا يردها حال الحرب لأنه إعانة لهم .اهـ. وأطلق في جواز صلح المرتدين وهو مقيد بما إذا غلبوا على بلدة وصار دارهم دار الحرب وإلا فلا، لأن فيه تقرير المرتد على الردة وذلك لا يجوز ولذا قيده الفقيه أبو الليث بما ذكرنا ؛ كذا في الفتح قوله : (ولم نبع سلاحاً منهم لأن النبي عليه السلام نهي عن بيع السلاح من أهل الحرب وحمله إليهم ولأن فيه تقويتهم على قتال المسلمين فيمنع من ذلك ، وصرح الشارحون بحرمته. أراد من السلاح ما يكون سبباً لتقويتهم على الحرب فدخل الكراع والحديد لأنه أصل السلاح وهو ظاهر الرواية . والكراع الخيل . ودخل الرقيق لأنهم يتوالدون عندهم فيعودون حرباً علينا مسلماً كان الرقيق أو كافراً. وخرج الطعام والقماش والقياس المنع إلا أنا عرفناه بالنص لأنه عليه السلام أمر ثمامة يمير أهل مكة وهم حرب عليه . وشمل كلامه ما قبل الموادعة وما بعدها لأنها على شرف الانقضاء أو النقض قال الفقيه أبو الليث : وليس هذا كما قالوا في بيع العصير ممن يجعله خمراً لأن العصير ليس بآلة للمعصية وإنما : خمراً، وأما هنا فالسلاح آلة للفتنة آلة لها بعدما يصير يصير في الحال اهـ. وفي كافي الحكم فإن كان الحربي جاء بسيف فاشترى مكانه قوساً أو رمحاً أو فرساً لم يترك أن يخرج به مكان سيفه، وكذا إذا استبدل بسيفه سيفاً خيراً منه، وإن كان مثله أو اهـ. فما يمنع المسلم منه المستأمن منهم أن يدخل به دارهم وإن خرج هو بشيء أن شراً منه لم يمنع ما ذكرنا فلا يمنع من الرجوع به إلا إذا أسلم العبد. هم بدؤوا بالغدر قبل مضي المدة فقاتلهم ولم ينبذ إليهم بل سأل الله تعالى أن يعمي عليهم حتى يبغتهم، وهذا هو المذكور لجميع أهل السير والمغازي ومن تلقى القصة وذكروها .
His saying: 'And we fight without renunciation if their king betrays, because they have become violators of the covenant, so there is no need to renounce it.' He made it absolute regarding the king's betrayal, so it includes whether it is by agreement of all or by the act of some with his permission, such that if a group of them with power enters the abode of Islam with his permission and fights the Muslims, it is a breach. And he restricted it to their king, because if a group enters without his permission, it is not breached with respect to all, but only breached with respect to the traitors, so that it is permissible to kill them and enslave them. And if they have no power, it is not a breach of the covenant. His saying: 'And the apostates without wealth, and if taken it is not returned' — i.e., we make peace with the apostates so that we may look into their affairs, because Islam is hoped from them, so it is permissible to delay fighting them in hope of their Islam. We do not take wealth for it because it is not permissible to take jizya from them, and if he took it, he does not return it because it is wealth without sanctity. And he indicated that making peace with the people of rebellion (ahl al-baghy) is all the more permissible, and nothing is taken from them. The commentator explicitly stated that their wealth is inviolable, so apparently if something is taken for the sake of peace, it is returned to them. In Fath al-Qadir: It is returned to them after the war lays down its burdens, and is not returned during war because it is assistance to them. And he spoke absolutely about the permissibility of making peace with apostates, but it is restricted to when they have overcome a town and their land has become dar al-harb; otherwise not, because it would be establishing the apostate upon apostasy, which is not permissible. Hence al-Faqih Abu al-Layth restricted it as we mentioned; thus in al-Fath. His saying: 'And we do not sell weapons to them because the Prophet ﷺ prohibited selling weapons to the people of war and carrying them to them, and because it strengthens them against the Muslims, so it is prevented. And the commentators stated that it is haram. He meant by weapons that which is a cause of strengthening them for war, so horses (al-kurā') and iron are included because they are the basis of weapons, and this is the apparent transmission. And slaves are included because they procreate among them and become a war against us, whether the slave is Muslim or disbeliever. Food and cloth are excluded, and analogically they would be prohibited except that we know it by the text, because the Prophet ﷺ commanded Thumama to supply food to the people of Makkah while they were at war with him. And his speech includes what is before the truce and after it, because it is on the verge of expiry or breach. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This is not like what they said about selling grape juice to one who turns it into wine, because grape juice is not a tool for sin, but here weapons are a tool for fitna [sedition] after it becomes [a weapon]. In Kafi al-Hakim: If the harbi came with a sword and bought a bow or spear or horse in its place, he is not left to take it out in place of his sword, and similarly if he exchanged his sword for a better sword, but if it is similar or worse, he is not prevented. So what prevents the Muslim from the musta'min allowing him to bring it into their land, and if he goes out with something [worse?] it does not prevent, he is not prevented from returning with it unless the slave becomes Muslim. They initiated treachery before the expiry of the period, so he fought them and did not renounce to them, but rather asked Allah to blind them so that he could take them by surprise, and this is what is mentioned by all the historians of expeditions and campaigns and those who related the story.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct rulings from the matn. First, 'we fight without renunciation if their king betrays' — because the king's betrayal itself breaks the covenant, making renunciation unnecessary. The betrayal includes an act by a group with the king's permission, but if a group acts without his permission, only those individuals are considered traitors; the truce with the rest remains intact. Second, 'the apostates without wealth' — a peace deal with apostates is allowed but only if they have seized a territory and become dominant; otherwise, it is forbidden as it would condone apostasy. No jizya can be taken from them, and wealth taken by mistake is not returned because it lacks sanctity. The commentator notes that a peace with rebels (ahl al-baghy) is even more permissible and their wealth is inviolable, so it must be returned after war. Third, 'we do not sell weapons to them' — this is haram. Weapons include anything that strengthens them militarily, such as horses, iron, and slaves (who may fight or produce offspring). Food and cloth are excluded due to the Prophet's command to Thumama. The prohibition applies both before and after a truce. The passage ends with a historical note: the Prophet fought the polytheists of Makkah without renunciation because they had already broken the treaty.

Key Terms
مرتدين (murtaddin): Apostates; people who left Islam. The ruling discusses making peace with them under certain conditions.
جزية (jizya): Poll tax imposed on non-Muslims under Muslim rule; not accepted from apostates.
أهل البغي (ahl al-baghy): Rebels; Muslims who revolt against the legitimate ruler. Their wealth is inviolable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for making peace with apostates?
A) They must pay jizya.
B) They must have overcome a town and become dar al-harb.
C) They must agree to return to Islam immediately.
D) No condition; peace is always allowed.

Key Takeaway
Peace with apostates is only permissible when they hold territory; otherwise, it is forbidden because it confirms apostasy.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Prohibition of Selling Weapons to the Enemy
Ruling on prohibition of selling weapons to the enemy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that selling weapons to the enemy is haram and identify which items are considered weapons (including horses, iron, and slaves) and which are excluded (food and cloth).

Core Idea
Selling weapons to the enemy is haram; includes horses, iron, and slaves; excludes food and cloth.

This passage addresses a crucial aspect of Muslim warfare ethics: the prohibition of selling weapons to the enemy. The Hanafi school defines 'weapons' broadly to include anything that strengthens the enemy militarily, such as horses, iron, and even slaves. The text also explains why food and cloth are excluded, based on a prophetic precedent. We will analyze the proof and the scope of the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
The text states: 'We do not sell weapons to them' and the commentators explicitly state it is haram. The proof is the Prophet's prohibition.
The prohibition includes both selling and carrying weapons to the enemy.
2
Define 'weapons' (silah)
Weapons are defined as anything that strengthens the enemy for war. This includes horses (kurā'), iron (hadid), and slaves (raqeeq).
Horses are the classical weapon; iron is the raw material; slaves are included because they can procreate and produce future warriors.
3
Identify excluded items
Food and cloth are excluded from the prohibition. Analogically, they would be banned, but a specific textual exception (the Prophet ordering Thumama to supply food to Mecca) overrides the analogy.
The exception is based on a specific hadith, not on general analogy.
4
Note the scope in time
The prohibition applies both before and after a truce, because a truce is on the verge of expiry or breach.
During a truce, weapons can still be used against Muslims if the truce breaks.
5
Address a potential analogy
Selling weapons is not like selling grape juice to a winemaker, because grape juice is not inherently a tool for sin, while weapons are tools for fitna (sedition/war).
This clarifies that the prohibition is based on the inherent nature of the object, not the intended use.

Key Terms
سلاح (silah): Weapons; includes horses (kurā'), iron (hadid), and slaves (raqeeq) because they contribute to warfare.
كراع (kurā'): Horses, specifically warhorses; included in the definition of weapons.
رقيق (raqeeq): Slaves; considered weapons because they can procreate and produce future warriors.
موادعة (muwada'ah): Truce or peace treaty; the prohibition applies before and after it.

Equal Sharing of Reinforcements and Helpers in Spoils
Explains equal sharing of reinforcements and helpers in spoils based on common cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that reinforcements and helpers share equally with original fighters and identify the cause (sabab) that justifies equal entitlement.

Core Idea
Reinforcements and helpers share equally in spoils because they share the same cause – crossing the border or witnessing the battle.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq explains a key principle in the law of spoils (ghanīma): reinforcements (al-madd) and helpers (al-rid') are entitled to an equal share alongside the original fighters. The commentator grounds this in the shared cause (sabab) – either crossing into enemy territory or personally witnessing the battle – and clarifies when this right begins and ends. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying the rules of distribution in military expeditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups and their ruling
Note the two groups: al-rid' (helpers) and al-madd (reinforcements). The passage states they share in the spoils because they are equal in the cause (sabab).
The sabab is either 'crossing the border' (mujāwaza) or 'witnessing the battle' (shuhūd al-waq'a).
2
Determine when the right to share begins
The right begins when reinforcements are present in Dār al-Ḥarb before the spoils are taken to Dār al-Islām (iḥrāz). They share with the original fighters on the same principle.
The key phrase: 'idhā al-madd fī Dār al-Ḥarb qabl an yukhrijū al-ghanīma ilā Dār al-Islām shārakūhum fīhā 'alā mā al-aṣl.
3
Identify when the right ends
The right ends by iḥrāz (securing spoils in Dār al-Islām), division by the imam in Dār al-Ḥarb, or sale of spoils therein. Each of these completes ownership, cutting off the partnership.
These three events: iḥrāz, qisma, bay' – are explicitly listed in the passage.
4
Apply the rule to non-combatants and commanders
The rule applies equally to fighters and non-fighters (e.g., those ill) and even to the army commander – no one is distinguished over another.
The passage says: 'al-muqātil wa ghayruh sawā'', citing Fatḥ al-Qadīr.
5
Confirm the absence of intra-madhhab disagreement
The commentator states this is without disagreement (bila khilāf) among the Hanafis, because all are equal in the cause of entitlement.
This strengthens the rule's authority within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وشرك يبيع الردء والمدد فيها أي في الغنيمة لاستوائهم في السبب وهو المجاوزة أو شهود الوقعة، وإذا المدد في دار الحرب قبل أن يخرجوا الغنيمة إلى دار الإسلام شاركوهم فيها على ما الأصل. وإنما ينقطع حق المشاركة عندنا بالإحراز أو بقسمة الإمام في دار الحرب قدمناه أو ببيعه المغانم فيها لأن بكل منها يتم الملك فتنقطع شركة المدد والردء ـ بكسر الراء وسكون الدال المهملة بعدها همزة - بمعنى العون والمدد الجماعة الناصرون للجند. وأفاد المصنف أن المقاتل وغيره سواء حتى يستحق الجندي الذي لم يقاتل لمرض أو غيره، وأنه لا يتميز على آخر بشيء حتى أمير العسكر وهذا بلا خلاف لاستواء الكل في سبب الاستحقاق ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي المحيط : المتطوع في الغزو وصاحب الديوان في الغنيمة سواء اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : إذا قسم الإمام الغنيمة ثم جاء رجل وادعى أنه شهد الوقعة وأقام عدلين فالقياس أن ينقض القسمة. وفي الاستحسان لا ينقض ويعوض من بيت المال قيمة نصيبه اهـ
His statement: 'and the share of the yabī' reinforcement and support in it' – meaning in the spoils, because they are equal in the cause, which is crossing the border or witnessing the battle. And when the reinforcement is in Dar al-Harb before they take out the spoils to Dar al-Islam, they share with them according to the principle. And the right of participation is cut off with us by iḥrāz (securing in Dar al-Islam) or by the imam's division in Dar al-Harb as we mentioned, or by his selling the spoils therein, because by each of these ownership is completed, so the partnership of the reinforcement and support is cut off. 'Al-rid' – with kasr of rā' and sukūn of dāl after it hamza – meaning help, and al-madd is the group of supporters for the army. And the author indicated that the fighter and non-fighter are equal, so that the soldier who did not fight due to illness or otherwise is entitled, and that no one is distinguished over another by anything, even the commander of the army, and this is without disagreement because all are equal in the cause of entitlement; as in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. And in al-Muḥīṭ: the volunteer in the raid and the holder of the register are equal in the spoils. And in al-Tatārkhāniyya: if the imam divided the spoils then a man came and claimed he witnessed the battle and brought two just witnesses, then by analogy the division is annulled, but by istiḥsān it is not annulled and he is compensated from the treasury the value of his share.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the statement from Kanz al-Daqā'iq regarding the share of reinforcements (al-madd) and helpers (al-rid') in the spoils of war. The commentator explains that both groups are considered equal to the original fighters in entitlement because they share the same cause: either crossing the border into enemy territory or actually witnessing the battle. The right to participate begins when they join in Dar al-Harb before the spoils are taken to Dar al-Islam, and it ends when the spoils are secured in Dar al-Islam (iḥrāz), or when the imam divides them in Dar al-Harb, or when he sells them there. The commentator clarifies that fighters and non-fighters (e.g., those ill) are equal, and even the commander is not distinguished. He then cites al-Muḥīṭ stating that a volunteer and a regular soldier are equal. Finally, he discusses a scenario from al-Tatārkhāniyya: if after division a claimant appears with witnesses that he was present, the qiyās would annul the division, but istiḥsān leaves it intact and compensates him from the treasury.

Key Terms
الردء (ar-rid'): Helpers who come to support the army.
المدد (al-madd): Reinforcements sent to aid the army.
الإحراز (al-iḥrāz): Securing spoils in Dar al-Islam, which completes ownership and cuts off the right to share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the cause (sabab) for entitlement to share in spoils according to this passage?
A) Actually fighting in the battle
B) Crossing the border or witnessing the battle
C) Being registered in the army diwan
D) Volunteering for the expedition

Key Takeaway
Reinforcements and helpers share equally in spoils because they share the cause (mujāwaza or shuhūd), and the right ends only by iḥrāz, division, or sale in Dar al-Harb.

Istihsan in Spoils Division: Claim After Distribution
Presents an istihsān rule overriding qiyās for claims after spoils division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school uses istihsān to override qiyās in the case of a claimant appearing after spoils have been divided.

Core Idea
Istihsan preserves completed divisions and compensates the claimant from the treasury, avoiding disorder in ownership.

This lesson focuses on a specific scenario detailed in the same passage: a man claims after the spoils have been divided that he witnessed the battle and brings two just witnesses. The passage contrasts the ruling by qiyās (annul the division) with the ruling by istihsān (do not annul, compensate from treasury). Understanding this distinction is crucial for appreciating how Hanafi fiqh balances strict analogy with practical considerations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
The imam has already divided the spoils. A man later comes and claims he was present at the battle, offering two just witnesses to prove it.
The claim is made after distribution is complete.
2
State the ruling by qiyās
By qiyās (strict analogy), the division should be annulled because the claimant's right was not satisfied – he should have been included.
Qiyās focuses on the claimant's individual entitlement.
3
State the ruling by istihsān
By istihsān (juristic preference), the division is not annulled. Instead, the claimant is compensated the value of his share from the public treasury (bayt al-māl).
Istihsān prioritizes stability and avoiding harm to the recipients.
4
Identify the reasoning behind istihsān
The istihsān reasoning is that annulling the division would cause disorder (ḍarar) and open the door to fraudulent claims after seeing the spoils. Compensation from the treasury is a fair middle ground.
This istihsān is based on consideration of public interest (maṣlaḥa).
5
Note the source and authority
The passage cites al-Tatārkhāniyya as the source of this ruling. It reflects the authority of later Hanafi fatwa works.
This is an example of fatawa mutākhkhirīn (later jurists) applying istihsān.

Source Text

القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning that would annul the division because an entitled person was missed.

بيت المال (bayt al-māl): The public treasury from which compensation is paid.

Key Takeaway

Selling Spoils Before Division: General Prohibition and Imam's Exception
States general prohibition and imam's exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling spoils before division and distinguish between the soldier's prohibition and the imam's permissible sale based on ijtihad.

Core Idea
Soldiers cannot sell spoils before division; the imam may sell for public interest.

This lesson examines the prohibition of selling spoils of war before they are distributed. You will learn the legal basis for the general rule and the important exception for the imam when he acts in the public interest. The analysis is drawn from the Hanafi commentary Al-Bahr al-Rā'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its basis
Read the source passage and determine the ruling for soldiers selling spoils before division. What is the hukm level? Note the two reasons given: lack of ownership before iḥrāz and uncertainty of share after iḥrāz.
The prohibition is supported by a prophetic tradition.
2
Locate the exception for the imam
Find the part where the imam's sale is discussed. What condition does al-Tahāwī set for its validity? Understand the role of ijtihad and public interest (maṣlaḥah).
The imam must see a genuine benefit, such as easing the burden of transport.
3
Contrast the two scenarios
Compare the soldier's situation (no ownership, unknown share) with the imam's authority. Why can the imam sell despite the general prohibition? Recognize the distinction based on capacity and ijtihad.
The imam represents the collective interest and exercises ijtihad.
4
Evaluate the ruling's authority
Identify the authority level: is this from Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah? Which later authorities confirm the imam's permissibility? Note the cross-madhhab difference.
Al-Tahāwī and al-Fatḥ al-Qadīr support the imam's sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبيعها قبلها) أي حرم بيع الغنائم قبل القسمة. أطلقه فشمل ما قبل الإحراز وما بعده أما قبله لم يملكه، وأما بعده فنصيبه مجهول فلا يمكنه أن وقد ورد النهي عن البيع قبل القسمة كما قدمناه. قوله : (وبيعها قبلها) قال في الفتح: وهذا في بيع الغزاة ظاهر، وأما بيع الإمام لها فذكر الطحاوي لأنه يصح مجتهد فيه يعني أنه لا بد أن يكون الإمام رأى المصلحة في ذلك وأقله تخفيف إكراه الحمل عن الناس أو عن البهائم ونحوه وتخفيف مؤنته عنهم فيقع عن اجتهاد في المصلحة فلا يقع جزافاً فينعقد بلا كراهة مطلقاً.
His statement: 'and its sale before it' – meaning it is forbidden to sell spoils before division. He made it absolute, so it includes before iḥrāz and after; before iḥrāz he does not own it, and after it his share is unknown, so he cannot [sell it] and the prohibition of sale before division has been reported as we have mentioned. His statement: 'and its sale before it' – al-Fath said: this is apparent regarding the sale by the soldiers, but regarding the imam's sale, al-Tahāwī mentioned that it is valid as ijtihād, meaning that the imam must see a public interest, the least of which is easing the burden of carrying for people or animals and reducing their expenses, so it is based on ijtihād in the interest, so it does not occur rashly, so it is contracted without any dislike.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This lesson addresses the ruling on selling spoils of war before they are divided among the soldiers. The commentator (author of al-Bahr al-Rā'iq) explains that such a sale is forbidden (haram) because ownership is not yet established: before iḥrāz, the spoils are not owned by anyone; after iḥrāz, each soldier's specific share is unknown, making the sale invalid due to uncertainty (gharar). The prohibition is supported by prophetic tradition. However, the commentator adds an important nuance from al-Fath: this applies to soldiers selling their expected shares. As for the imam (leader), he may sell the spoils before division if he sees a clear public interest, such as reducing the burden of transporting heavy items. This sale is based on ijtihād and is not disliked. Thus, the ruling distinguishes between individual soldiers and the imam.

Key Terms
الغنيمة (al-ghanīmah): Spoils taken from disbelievers by force of arms.
القسمة (al-qismah): Division of spoils among those entitled.
الإحراز (al-iḥrāz): Bringing spoils into safe custody (in Dār al-Islām).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is a soldier's sale of spoils before division prohibited even after iḥrāz?
A) Because the spoils are still in the enemy's land
B) Because his specific share is unknown (majhūl)
C) Because he did not participate in the battle
D) Because the imam's permission is not obtained

Key Takeaway
Selling spoils before division is generally prohibited for soldiers due to lack of ownership or unknown share, but the imam may sell if he exercises ijtihād for a genuine public interest.

The Marketman's Share in Spoils: Fighting as the Condition
Ruling with proof: marketman's share conditional on fighting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a marketman's share in spoils, explain the condition of fighting, and apply the same reasoning to analogous cases.

Core Idea
A marketman has no share in spoils unless he fights — intention alone is insufficient.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on the marketman (as-sūqī), a trader who accompanies the army. The key principle is that entitlement to spoils is based on the actual cause (fighting), not the apparent cause (crossing the border). We'll see how this ruling is derived and how it applies to other scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage states that the marketman gets no share (neither a regular share nor a token) unless he fights. Identify this as the Hanafi position.
2
Analyze the ta'lil (legal reasoning)
The reason given: crossing into enemy territory with the intention of fighting is the apparent cause, but since the marketman's intention is trade, the real cause (actual fighting) must be present for entitlement.
This is a shift from apparent cause to real cause.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is from the general practice (sunnah ahad) of the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم that spoils are distributed based on actual fighting. The proof is not explicitly quoted but implied.
The dalil has zanni thubut and zanni dalala.
4
Apply the ruling to analogous cases
The commentator expands: a Harbī who becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb, or an apostate who returns to Islam and joins the army, also only gets a share if they fight. Apply the same reasoning here.
These cases reinforce the principle.
5
Note the exception
If the marketman fights, his trade is secondary and does not harm his entitlement, just as a pilgrim who trades on Hajj does not lose his reward.
The exception shows that the real cause (fighting) overrides the apparent one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا لسوقي (بلا (قتال أي لا شركة للسوقي في الغنيمة إذا لم يقاتل لا سهماً ولا رضخاً لأنه لم توجد المجاوزة على قصد القتال فانعدم السبب الظاهر فيعتبر السبب الحقيقي وهو القتال فيقيد الاستحقاق على حسب حاله فارساً أو راجلاً عند القتال. وأشار المصنف إلى أن الحربي إذا أسلم في دار الحرب أو المرتد إذا أسلم ولحق بالجيش لا يستحق شيئاً إن لم يقاتل، صرح به في المحيط. وذكر الشارح أن السوقي إذا قاتل ظهر أن قصده القتال والتجارة تبع له فلا يضره كالحاج إذا اتجر في طريق الحج لا ينقص أجره اهـ
His statement: 'not for the marketman without fighting' – meaning no partnership for the marketman in the spoils if he did not fight, neither a share nor a token (radkh), because crossing the border with the intention of fighting did not occur, so the apparent cause is absent, so the real cause is considered, which is fighting, so entitlement is restricted according to his condition as cavalry or infantry at the time of fighting. And the author indicated that a Harbī (enemy combatant) if he becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb, or an apostate if he becomes Muslim and joins the army, does not deserve anything if he did not fight, as stated in al-Muḥīṭ. And the commentator mentioned that if the marketman fights, it becomes clear that his intention was fighting and trade is secondary, so it does not harm him, like the pilgrim who trades on the way of Hajj, his reward is not diminished.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling regarding a marketman (sūqī) – a trader who accompanies the army. The commentator explains that such a person does not receive any share of the spoils, not even a small token (radkh), unless he actually fights. The reason is that the apparent cause of entitlement is crossing into enemy territory with the intention of fighting; the marketman's intention is trade, so the real cause (actual fighting) must be present. If he does fight, his intention is deemed primarily for fighting, with trade being secondary, so he qualifies for a share based on his fighting status (cavalry or infantry). The commentator also applies this logic to similar cases: a Harbī who accepts Islam in Dar al-Harb or an apostate who returns to Islam and joins the army only gets a share if they fight. The commentary concludes with an analogy: just as a pilgrim who trades on the way does not lose his reward, the marketman who fights does not lose his entitlement.

Key Terms
السوقي (as-sūqī): A trader who accompanies the army for commerce.
الرضخ (ar-radkh): A small token given to non-fighters like women, children, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a marketman fights?
A) He still gets no share because his primary intention is trade
B) He gets a share based on his fighting status (cavalry or infantry)
C) He gets only a token (radkh)
D) He gets a full share equal to a regular soldier regardless of his fighting role

Key Takeaway
A marketman's entitlement to spoils is conditional on actual fighting; trade intentions do not entitle him without combat.

The Share of a Horseman: Abu Hanifa vs the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute with dalil naqli and ta'lil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of shares for a horseman according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, identify the textual and rational proofs for each, and explain the Hanafi method of resolving contradictory evidence.

Core Idea
When conflicting prophetic actions contradict, the Prophet's explicit statement takes precedence over his action.

In the division of booty (ghanimah), the Imam must distribute shares among the warriors. A key intra-Hanafi disagreement concerns how many shares a horseman receives. Imam Abu Hanifa holds two shares, while his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue for three. This lesson unpacks the textual and rational evidence behind each position and shows how the Hanafi school resolves such disputes using usul al-fiqh principles.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling attributed to Abu Hanifa: a foot soldier gets one share, a horseman two shares. Note that this is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that a horseman gets three shares. This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn rely on a hadith from Ibn Umar that the Prophet assigned three shares to a horseman, and a rational argument that a horseman has three functions (charge, retreat, stand firm) requiring triple capability.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa uses a hadith from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet gave two shares, and argues that the Prophet's explicit statement (two shares) outweighs his action (three shares) when they conflict. Moreover, Ibn Umar's own narration also reports two shares, so his conflicting reports are set aside. Rational: the horseman's two functions (charge and retreat) are of one kind, so only one extra share is justified; the horseman has two causes (soul and horse) versus one for the foot soldier, so double share is appropriate.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is in the distribution: under Abu Hanifa, each horseman gets 2 shares; under the Sahibayn, 3 shares. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في كيفية القسمة أفردها بفصل على حدة لكثرة شعبها، والقسمة جمع نصيب شائع في معين. قال الشارح: يجب على الإمام أن يقسم الغنيمة ويخرج خمسها لقوله تعالى فإن الله خمسه ويقسم أربعة الأخماس على الغانمين للنصوص الواردة فيه وعليه إجماع المسلمين. وفي التتارخانية: ينبغي للإمام إذا أراد الدخول بدار الحرب أن يعرض العسكر ليعرف عددهم راجلهم وفارسهم ويكتب أسماءهم، فمن كتب اسمه فارساً ثم مات فرسه بعدما جاوز الدرب استحق سهم الفارس ولو باعها لا يستحق إلا أن يستبدل فرساً آخر. قوله: (للراجل سهم وللفارس سهمان) يعني عند أبي حنيفة وقالا: للفارس ثلاثة أسهم لما روى ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما أن النبي الله وأسهم للفارس ثلاثة أسهم وللراجل سهماً، ولأن الاستحقاق بالكفاية وهي على ثلاثة أمثال الراجل لأنه للكر والفر والثبات والراجل للثبات لا غير. ولأبي حنيفة ما روى ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أعطى للفارس سهمين وللراجل سهماً فتعارض فعلاه فيرجع إلى قوله وقد قال عليه السلام «للفارس سهمان وللراجل سهم» كيف وقد روي عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قسم للفارس سهمين، وإذا تعارضت روايتاه ترجحت رواية غيره، ولأن الكر والفر من جنس واحد فيكون غناؤه مثل غناء الراجل فيفضل عليه بسهم، ولأنه تعذر اعتبار مقدار الزيادة لتعذر معرفته فيدار الحكم على سبب ظاهر وللفارس سببان النفس والفرس وللراجل سبب واحد فكان استحقاقه على ضعفه؛ كذا في الهداية. وتعقبه في العناية بأن طريقة استدلاله مخالفة لقواعد الأصول فإن الأصل الدليلين إذا تعارضا وتعذر التوفيق والترجيح يصار إلى ما بعده لا إلى ما قبله وهو قال فتعارض فعلاه فيرجع إلى قوله والمسلك المعهود في مثله أن يستدل بقوله ويقول فعله لا يعارض قوله لأن القول أولى بالاتفاق. وقد تقدم نظيره في باب سجود السهو. وفي المحيط والفارس في السفينة في البحر يستحق سهمين وإن لم يمكنه القتال على الفرس في السفينة لأنه إن لم يباشر القتال على الفرس فقد تأهب للقتال على الفرس والمتأهب للشيء كالمباشر. أطلق في الفارس وهو من معه فرس فشمل الفرس المملوك والمستأجر والمستعار والمغصوب إذا لم يسترده، فإن استرده صاحبه قبل المقاتلة فسيأتي.
A chapter on the method of division. He devoted a separate section to it due to its many branches. Division is the collection of a common share in a specific thing. The commentator says: It is obligatory upon the Imam to divide the booty and take out its fifth due to His statement, the Exalted: '...then indeed for Allah is its fifth...' [al-Anfal 8:41] and he divides the four-fifths among the warriors due to the texts transmitted concerning it, and upon it is the consensus of the Muslims. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: It is befitting for the Imam, when he intends to enter the abode of war, to review the army to know their number, their foot soldiers and horsemen, and to write their names. Whoever is registered as a horseman, then his horse dies after passing the trench, he is entitled to the share of a horseman; if he sells it, he is not entitled unless he obtains another horse. His statement: (For the foot soldier is one share, and for the horseman is two shares) means according to Abu Hanifa. And the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: For the horseman is three shares due to what Ibn 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both, narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, assigned three shares for the horseman and one share for the foot soldier, and because entitlement is based on sufficiency, and that is three times the foot soldier because the horseman is for charging, retreating, and standing firm, while the foot soldier is only for standing firm. For Abu Hanifa is what Ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, gave the horseman two shares and the foot soldier one share. So their two actions contradict, so recourse is to his statement, and he, peace be upon him, said: 'For the horseman are two shares and for the foot soldier one share.' How? And it has been narrated from Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet, peace be upon him, divided for the horseman two shares. So if his two narrations conflict, the narration of another is preponderated. And because charging and retreating are of one kind, so his effectiveness is like the effectiveness of the foot soldier, so he is given extra by one share. And because it is difficult to determine the amount of increase due to the difficulty of knowing it, so the ruling is made to revolve around an apparent cause, and for the horseman there are two causes: the soul and the horse, and for the foot soldier there is one cause, so his entitlement is double. Thus in al-Hidaya. And it was criticized in al-'Inaya that his method of deduction contradicts the principles of usul, for the principle when two evidences conflict and reconciliation and preponderance are impossible is to resort to what is after it, not to what is before it. He said: So their two actions contradict, so recourse is to his statement, and the known path in such a case is to deduce from his statement and say: his action does not contradict his statement because the statement is more worthy by agreement. And its like has preceded in the chapter on the prostration of forgetfulness. And in al-Muhit: The horseman on a ship at sea is entitled to two shares, even if he cannot fight on the horse on the ship, because if he does not directly fight on the horse, he has prepared himself for fighting on the horse, and one who prepares for something is like one who does it. He used the absolute term 'horseman', which is one who has a horse, so it includes a horse that is owned, rented, borrowed, or usurped if he has not taken it back; if its owner takes it back before the battle, then it will come later.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the division of booty, specifically the shares of foot soldiers and horsemen. The author begins by explaining the general principles of dividing booty: the Imam must extract one-fifth (khums) and distribute the remaining four-fifths among the warriors, based on the Qur'anic verse and consensus. He then cites a practical ruling from al-Tatarkhaniyya about registering soldiers and the entitlement to a horseman's share if the horse dies after crossing the trench. The core ruling is attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa: a foot soldier (rajil) gets one share (sahm), and a horseman (faris) gets two shares. The two companions (Sahibayn), Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree, holding that the horseman gets three shares. Their evidence is a hadith from Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet assigned three shares to the horseman and one to the foot soldier. They also argue by reason: the horseman's role includes charging, retreating, and standing firm, requiring three times the capability of a foot soldier who only stands firm. Abu Hanifa relies on a hadith from Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet gave two shares to the horseman and one to the foot soldier. The author explains the juristic method: when actions of the Prophet conflict, recourse is taken to his statements, and the Prophet explicitly said, 'For the horseman are two shares and for the foot soldier one share.' Furthermore, the narration from Ibn 'Umar is reportedly inconsistent, so the other narration is preferred. Additional rational arguments are presented: the effectiveness of the horseman in charging and retreating is similar to that of the foot soldier in fighting, so the horseman is only given one extra share; and since the exact increase is indeterminate, the ruling is based on the apparent cause—the horseman has two means (soul and horse) while the foot soldier has one, so entitlement is double. The text then notes a critique from al-'Inaya, which states that the method of deduction in al-Hidaya contradicts usul al-fiqh because when textual evidences conflict and reconciliation is impossible, the proper approach is to look to what is after (e.g., other principles), not to claim that action contradicts statement. The commentator argues that the correct path is to use the Prophet's statement as primary. Finally, the passage extends the ruling: a horseman on a ship at sea also gets two shares, even if he cannot fight on the horse, because he has prepared to fight on it. The definition of 'horseman' includes one who has a horse, whether owned, rented, borrowed, or usurped, provided the horse is not reclaimed before the battle.

Key Terms
راجل (rājil): Foot soldier who fights on foot in the battle.
فارس (fāris): Horseman who fights mounted on a horse.
سهم (sahm): A share or portion of the booty allocated to a warrior.
غنيمة (ghanīmah): Booty taken from the enemy by force during battle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One share
B) Two shares
C) Three shares
D) Four shares

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when a prophetic action and statement conflict, the statement takes precedence; the horseman receives two shares over the foot soldier's one.

Shares for Two Horses: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether one gets shares for two horses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding shares for multiple horses in jihad and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Only one horse share in spoils because effective cause is actual combat on one horse at a time.

This lesson delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about spoils distribution for a fighter who enters battle with two horses. The question is whether he receives shares for both horses or only one. The text presents the positions of the three major Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Abu Yusuf. By analyzing their proofs and counter-arguments, we will understand the school's final relied-upon position and the legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State clearly what each side holds: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say one share for the horses; Abu Yusuf says two shares.
Abu Yusuf is the opposing school here, but it's an intra-Hanafi disagreement.
2
Examine the evidence for each
Identify the hadith and rational arguments cited by each side. Note the difference in the interpretation of the same or different narrations.
The key hadith is of Bara' ibn Aws for the majority, and another hadith for Abu Yusuf.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Explain how the majority (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad) refute Abu Yusuf's evidence by reinterpreting his hadith as tanfil (an extra grant) and by using qiyas on the impossibility of fighting on two horses simultaneously.
Also note the analogy with the case of Salama ibn al-Akwa'.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: if a fighter has two horses and fights with both, Abu Yusuf gives two horse shares, while Abu Hanifa/Muhammad give one.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Conclude that the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is relied upon (mufta bihi) as the majority view and consistent with the principle of causality (ta'lil).
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو له فرسان) يعني لو كان له فرسان لا يستحق إلا سهمين فلا إلا يسهم لفرس واحدة. وقال أبو يوسف : يسهم لفرسين لما روي أنه عليه السلام أسهم لفرسين ولأن الواحد قد يعي فيحتاج إلى الآخر. ولهما أن البراء بن أوس قاد فرسين فلم يسهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا لفرس، ولأن القتال لا يتحقق بفرسين دفعة واحدة فلا يكون السبب الظاهر مفضياً إلى القتال عليهما فيسهم لواحد ولهذا لا يسهم لثلاثة أفراس، وما رواه محمول على التنفيل كما أعطى سلمة بن الأكوع رضي الله عنه سهمين وهو راجل. وفي النهاية: وهذه المسألة نظير ما بيننا في النكاح أن المرأة لا تستحق النفقة إلا لخادم واحد عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف : تستحق النفقة الخادمين
His statement: (And if he has two horses) means if he has two horses, he does not deserve except two shares? No, he only gets a share for one horse. Abu Yusuf says: He gets shares for two horses, because it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave shares for two horses, and because one horse may tire and he needs the other. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that Bara' ibn Aws led two horses, so the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) only gave a share for one horse, and because fighting cannot be realized with two horses at once, so the apparent cause does not lead to fighting on both, so he gives a share for one, and for this reason he does not give shares for three horses. And what he narrated is interpreted as a gift (tanfil), just as he gave Salama ibn al-Akwa' (may Allah be pleased with him) two shares while he was a foot soldier. And in al-Nihaya: This issue is analogous to what is between us in marriage: the woman is not entitled to maintenance except for one servant according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf says: She is entitled to maintenance for two servants.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the distribution of spoils for a person who enters enemy territory with two horses. The question is: does he receive shares for both horses or only one? Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that he only gets one share, while Imam Abu Yusuf argues for two shares. The evidence for Abu Yusuf includes a narration that the Prophet (ﷺ) awarded shares for two horses, and a rational argument that a single horse may tire, necessitating a backup. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) counter with a narration of Bara' ibn Aws, who led two horses but only received one share. They also argue that fighting cannot be done on two horses simultaneously, so the apparent cause (the horse being used in combat) is only fulfilled by one. They interpret Abu Yusuf's narration as a case of tanfil (an extra grant) rather than a standard share, analogous to the Prophet's giving two shares to Salama ibn al-Akwa' while he was on foot. The passage ends by comparing this dispute to a similar one in marriage law regarding maintenance for servants, reinforcing the methodological consistency within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الفرس (al-faras): A horse used for fighting in jihad.
التنفيل (al-tanfil): An extra grant or reward given beyond the regular share of spoils.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, how many shares does a fighter get for two horses?
A) Two shares
B) One share
C) Three shares
D) No shares

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, entitlement to a horse share in spoils is based on the effective cause of actual combat, which can only be realized on one horse at a time, hence only one share for multiple horses.

Baradhin (Non-Arab Horses) Equal to Arab Horses
Ruling that non-Arab horses get same share as Arab horses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that baradhin (non-Arab horses) receive an equal share in spoils as 'itāq (Arab horses), explain the Quranic and qiyas proof, and apply the 'illa of irhab to similar cases.

Core Idea
All horses are equal for spoils; the Quran's generic 'khayl' covers both Arab and non-Arab breeds.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling that non-Arab horses (baradhin) are treated equally to purebred Arab horses when distributing shares of spoils (ghanima). The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq cites the Quranic verse (al-Anfal 60) and uses qiyas based on the effective cause of causing terror (irhab). We will walk through the proof and see how the generality of the Quranic term and the common military utility lead to equal treatment.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify that baradhin (non-Arab horses) receive the same share in spoils as 'itāq (Arab horses).
2
Identify the Quranic Dalil
Locate the verse (Anfal 60) and note that the term 'khayl' includes all horse types.
The verse mentions 'ribāt al-khayl' — tethering horses for terror.
3
Analyze the Qiyas Argument
Understand that the effective cause ('illa) is causing terror (irhab), which is present in both Arab and non-Arab horses due to military utility.
Arab horses are faster, but baradhin are more patient and gentle — both are fit for combat.
4
Compare Competing Qualities
The text balances the speed of Arab horses with the endurance of baradhin, concluding both have a beneficial quality so they are equal.
This is a form of qiyas where the presence of the 'illa in both yields the same ruling.
5
Conclude the Hanafi Position
Affirm that the Hanafi school relies on the generality of the Quranic wording and the shared 'illa, making the ruling mufta bihi.
No significant intra-madhhab difference is recorded here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والبراذين) كالعتاق لأن الإرهاب مضاف إلى جنس الخيل في الكتاب قال الله تعالى : (ومن رباط الخيل ترهبون به عدوا لله وعدوكم) [الأنفال : 60] واسم الخيل ينطلق على البراذين والعراب والهجين والمقرف إطلاقاً واحداً، ولأن العربي إن كان في الطلب والهرب أقوى فالبرذون أصبر وألين عطفاً ففي كل منهما منفعة معتبرة فاستويا والبرذون التركي من الخيل والجمع البراذين وخلافها العراب والأنثى برذونة، وعتاق الخيل والطير كرائمها؛ كذا في المغرب. وفي شرح النقاية : العتاق - بكسر العين - كرام الخيل العربية والبراذين خيل العجم والهجين الذي أبوه عربي عجمية والمقرف عكسه
His statement: (And baradhin) are like 'itāq because terror (irhab) is attributed to the genus of horses in the Book. Allah Most High said: 'And from the tethering of horses you terrify thereby the enemy of Allah and your enemy' (Anfal 60). And the name 'khayl' (horses) applies to baradhin, 'irab (Arabian), hajin, and muqraf uniformly. And because the Arabian horse is stronger in pursuit and escape, the baradhun is more patient and gentler in disposition, so each has a beneficial quality considered, so they are equal. The baradhun is a Turkish horse, plural baradhin; its opposite is 'irab; female is baradhuna. 'Itāq of horses and birds are their noble ones; likewise in al-Mughrib. And in Sharh al-Niqaya: al-'itāq (with kasr of 'ayn) are noble Arabian horses, and baradhin are Persian (non-Arab) horses; hajin is one whose father is Arab and mother non-Arab; muqraf is its opposite.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage expands the ruling on spoils shares for horses to include baradhin (non-Arab, specifically Turkish or Persian horses) as being equivalent to 'itāq (purebred Arab horses). The proof is from the Quranic verse (8:60), which mentions 'ribāt al-khayl' (tethering horses) as a means of terrorizing enemies. The term 'khayl' (horses) is a general term that includes all types: Arab, Turkish (baradhin), crossbreeds (hajin and muqraf). The author provides two reasons for equality: (1) the Quranic attribution of terror to the genus of horses, not a specific type; (2) although Arab horses excel in speed, baradhin are more patient and gentle, so both have military utility. Thus, they are treated equally for the purpose of receiving a share (sahm) in the spoils. The text also defines the terminology: 'itāq are noble Arab horses, baradhin are non-Arab (Persian or Turkish), hajin has an Arab father and non-Arab mother, and muqraf is the reverse.

Key Terms
البراذين (al-baradhin): Non-Arab (Persian/Turkish) horses, as distinguished from purebred Arab horses.
العتاق (al-'itāq): Noble, purebred Arabian horses.
الإرهاب (al-irhab): Terrorizing the enemy, the underlying rationale for giving a share for horses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The Quranic verse mentioning 'khayl' generally
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Analogy to mules

Key Takeaway
Baradhin (non-Arab horses) receive the same share in spoils as Arab horses because the Quranic term 'khayl' includes all horses and the 'illa of irhab is present in both.

The Ruling on Shares for Mules and Dromedaries in War Spoils
Ruling on spoils shares for mules and dromedaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on spoils shares for mules and riding camels and explain the rationale of causing terror.

Core Idea
Only horses used for fighting qualify for a share; mules and dromedaries do not.

In the Hanafi school, the allocation of shares in spoils of war is tied to the ability to cause terror (irhab) in combat. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clearly states that mules and riding camels (al-rahila) do not receive a share because they are not used for fighting. Let's unpack the ruling and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states that mules (al-baghl) and dromedaries (al-rahila) are not entitled to a share in spoils. They are explicitly excluded.
2
Locate the proof
The reason given is 'because terror is not achieved through them, as one does not fight upon them.' This is the dalil (evidence).
The text uses qiyas implicitly: horses cause terror through fighting, mules do not.
3
Understand the effective cause (illa)
The illa for entitlement is causing terror (irhab) by fighting on the animal. Horses are used for charging and combat, achieving terror. Mules and camels are used for transport, not fighting, so they do not achieve this.
The illa is derived from the text's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا الراحلة والبغل أي لا يكونان كالعتاق فلا لهما لأن الإرهاب لا يقع بهما إذ لا يقاتل عليهما
His statement: (Not the dromedary and the mule) i.e., they are not like 'itāq (horses), so they do not get shares, because terror is not achieved through them, as one does not fight upon them.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This is a brief ruling stating that dromedaries (al-rahila: riding camels) and mules (al-baghl) are not entitled to a share in the spoils of war, unlike horses. The reason given is that these animals are not used for direct combat; they are for transportation, not for fighting. The effective cause ('illa) for entitlement is causing terror (irhab) to the enemy, which is achieved through fighting on horseback, not on mules or camels. Therefore, they are excluded from receiving a share.

Key Terms
الراحلة (al-rahila): A riding camel, used for travel, not for combat.
البغل (al-baghl): A mule, offspring of a horse and donkey, used for transport.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The animal's speed
B) The animal's value
C) The animal's use in combat to cause terror
D) The animal's breed

Key Takeaway
Only animals used for fighting and causing terror, like horses, qualify for spoils shares; mules and dromedaries do not.
