Does Irrigation Affect Land Tax Classification?
Critique of a condition from al-Jami' al-Saghir regarding land classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether irrigation method determines if land left to non-Muslims is kharaji or 'ushri, and distinguish the view of Muhammad al-Shaybani from the majority.

Core Idea
Owner's status trumps irrigation: non-Muslim-owned land is always kharaji, never 'ushri.

The text discusses a qualification found in al-Jami' al-Saghir regarding land left to its original non-Muslim inhabitants after conquest. Al-Quduri stated it absolutely as kharaji, but the condition in al-Jami' suggests that river water must reach the land for it to be kharaji. The commentator al-Zayla'i finds this problematic because the owners are disbelievers, and according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the status of the owner determines the land classification, not the irrigation source. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi debate and clarifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute's core
Delineate the question: Does the irrigation method (river water vs. rain/spring) affect whether land left to non-Muslims is classified as kharaji (subject to land tax) or 'ushri (subject to tithe)?
Both sides agree on the basic rule: conquered land left to non-Muslims is kharaji. The dispute is about an exception.
2
State the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf)
They hold that such land is always kharaji regardless of irrigation. The owner's disbelief is the sole determinant; even if a disbeliever acquires 'ushri land, it converts to kharaji. Therefore, initially imposing 'ushr on a kafir is invalid.
This position is based on the principle that 'ushr is only for Muslim-owned land.
3
Present Muhammad al-Shaybani's view and his evidence
According to a report in al-Jami' al-Saghir, if river water does not reach the land and it is irrigated by springs or rain, it is 'ushri. He ties the tax to the land's productive capacity, which depends on water.
Even Muhammad does not allow initial 'ushr on kafir-owned land; his condition applies only after the land is already classified.
4
Analyze the hanafi response and critique
The commentator rejects the condition because it contradicts the established rule. The text from al-Jami' is not as clear as in al-Hidayah. Fath al-Qadir concludes that land conquered by force and left to non-Muslims is always kharaji, even if rain-fed.
This is the mufta bihi position.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In the majority view, such land is always kharaji; in Muhammad's view, it could be 'ushri if not river-irrigated, but even he would not impose 'ushr initially. The practical difference is narrow, but the principle is important for land tax systems.
The relied-upon view simplifies implementation: always kharaji.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق المصنف فيما أقر أهله عليه تبعاً للقدوري، وقيده في الجامع الصغير على ما في الهداية بأن يصل إليها ماء الأنهار لتكون خراجية، وما لم يصل إليها ماء الأنهار واستخرج منها عين فهي أرض عشر لأن العشر يتعلق بالأراضي النامية ونماؤها بمائها فيعتبر السقي بماء العشر أو بماء الشعر أو بماء الخراج اهـ. وهو مشكل لأنا نقطع بأن الأرض التي أقر أهلها عليها لو كانت تسقى بعين أو بماء السماء لم تكن إلا خراجية لأن أهلها كفار والكفار لو انتقلت إليهم أرض عشرية ومعلوم أن العشرية قد تسقى بعين أو بماء السماء لا تبقى على العشرية بل تصير خراجية في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد، فكيف يبتدأ الكافر بتوظيف العشر؟ ثم كونها عشرية عند محمد إذا انتقلت إليه كذلك أما في الابتداء فهو أيضاً يمنعه، والعبارة التي نقلها عن الجامع في غاية البيان ليست كما في الهداية، وقد أطال المحقق في فتح القدير في تقريره ثم قال : والحاصل أن التي فتحت عنوة إن أقر الكفار عليها لا يوظف عليهم إلا الخراج ولو سقيت بماء المطر، وإن قسمت بين المسلمين لا يوظف إلا العشر وإن سقيت بماء الأنهار.
The author made it absolute in what its inhabitants were left upon, following al-Qudūrī, but it is qualified in al-Jāmiʻ al-Ṣaghīr according to what is in al-Hidāyah: that the water of rivers reaches it for it to be kharājī; and if the water of rivers does not reach it and a spring is extracted from it, then it is tithe land, because the tithe relates to productive lands and their growth is through their water. So consideration is given to irrigation with tithe water, or with well water, or with kharāj water. End. But it is problematic because we are certain that the land whose inhabitants were left upon, if it were irrigated by a spring or by rainwater, it would only be kharājī, because its inhabitants are disbelievers. If a tithe land transferred to them—and it is known that tithe land may be irrigated by spring or rainwater—it does not remain tithe land but becomes kharājī according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. So how can a disbeliever be initially taxed with tithe? Moreover, it being tithe land according to Muhammad if it transfers to him is similar; but in the beginning, he also prevents it. The expression quoted from al-Jāmiʻ is not as clear as in al-Hidāyah. The investigator in Fatḥ al-Qadīr elaborated in his determination, then he said: The conclusion is that what is conquered by force: if the disbelievers are left upon it, nothing is imposed on them except kharāj, even if irrigated by rainwater; and if it is divided among the Muslims, nothing is imposed except tithe, even if irrigated by river water.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The text discusses a condition mentioned in al-Jāmiʻ al-Ṣaghīr that for land left to non-Muslim inhabitants to be kharājī, river water must reach it; if irrigated by springs or rain, it would be ʻushrī. The commentator al-Zaylaʻī finds this problematic because the inhabitants are disbelievers, and according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a disbeliever obtains an ʻushrī land, it becomes kharājī due to the owner's status, even if irrigation is from rain. So initially, a disbeliever cannot be subject to ʻushr. Muhammad al-Shaybānī differs, but even in his view, initial taxation with ʻushr for a kāfir is not allowed. The correct view, as concluded in Fatḥ al-Qadīr, is that land conquered by force and left to non-Muslims is always kharājī regardless of irrigation; if divided among Muslims, it is always ʻushrī regardless of irrigation.

Key Terms
أقر أهله عليه (uqirra ahluhu ʻalayhi): The inhabitants were left upon the land, i.e., allowed to stay after conquest.
خراج (kharāj): Land tax imposed on non-Muslim owners of conquered land.
عشر (ʻushr): Tithe (10% or 5% of produce) levied on Muslim-owned agricultural land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi Hanafi position, what is the classification of land conquered by force and left to its non-Muslim inhabitants if it is irrigated by rainfall?
A) It is ʻushrī because it is rain-fed.
B) It is kharājī because the owners are non-Muslims.
C) It is ʻushrī because it is productive land.
D) It is exempt from both taxes.

Key Takeaway
Land left to non-Muslim inhabitants after conquest is always kharaji according to the majority Hanafi view; irrigation method is irrelevant for classification.

Egyptian Lands: The Ruling That They Are Rental, Not Kharaj
Fatwa that what is taken from Egyptian lands is rental, not kharaj, due to owners dying out

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that Egyptian lands are subject to rental (badal ijara) rather than kharaj, and explain the reason (death of original owners without heirs).

Core Idea
Egyptian lands lost kharaj status; they are Bayt al-Mal property cultivated under rental.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq addresses a common misconception: the payments taken from Egyptian lands are not classical kharaj but a rental substitute. The original landowners gradually died out without heirs, so the lands became property of the Bayt al-Mal. This ruling has major implications for land ownership, taxation, and endowments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the payment
Ibn Nujaym quotes Ibn al-Humam stating that the amount collected from Egyptian lands is badal ijara (rental substitution), not kharaj. Examine the phrase 'إنما هو بدل إجارة' and note the contrast with kharaj.
The key shift: from obligatory land tax to rental agreement.
2
Understand the cause
The reason given is that the original owners died gradually without leaving heirs ('لموت المالكين شيئاً فشيئاً من غير إخلاف ورثة'). As a result, the lands reverted to Bayt al-Mal.
This is a historical contingency, not a change in law.
3
Recognize the legal consequence
Since the land is now Bayt al-Mal property, the Imam acts as guardian. The payment is compensation for use, not a religious levy. Hence, if someone buys the land from Bayt al-Mal, no kharaj is due.
The land becomes like residential property (بمنزلة دور السكنى).
4
Apply to waqf
If the land is then endowed (waqf), it remains free of kharaj because the Imam already took the compensation for the Muslims. The author refutes attempts to impose kharaj on waqf lands using this reasoning.
This protects endowments from additional fiscal burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتح القدير: المأخوذ الآن أراضي مصر إنما هو بدل إجارة لاخراج ألا ترى أن الأراضي ليست مملوكة للزراع، وهذا بعدما قلنا إن أرض مصر خراجية والله أعلم كأنه لموت المالكين شيئاً فشيئاً من غير إخلاف ورثة فصارت لبيت المال. وينبغي على هذا أن لا يصح بيع الإمام ولا شراؤه من وكيل بيت المال لشيء منها لأن نظره في مال المسلمين كنظره في مال اليتيم فلا يجوز له عقاره إلا بيع من ضرورة عدم وجود ما ينفقه سواه فلذا كتبت في فتوى رفعت إلي في شراء السلطان الأشرف برسباي الأرض ممن ولاه نظر بيت المال هل يجوز شراؤه منه وهو الذي ولاه؟ فكتبت: إذا كان بالمسلمين حاجة والعياذ بالله تعالى جاز ذلك اهـ . كأنه أجاب لا يجوز كما لا يخفى وهو مبني على قول المتقدمين، أما على قول المتأخرين المفتى به لا ينحصر جواز بيع عقار اليتيم فيما ذكر بل فيه وفيما إذا كان على الميت دين لا وفاء له إلا منه أو رغب فيه بضعف قيمته فكذلك نقول للإمام بيع العقار لغير حاجة إذا رغب فيه بضعف قيمته على المفتى به، وهذه مسألة مهمة وقع النزاع فيها في زماننا في تفتيش وقع من نائب مصر على الرزق في سنة ثمان وخمسين وتسعمائة حتى ادعى بعضهم بأن المبايعات للأراضي من بيت المال غير صحيحة ليتوصل بذلك إلى إبطال الأوقاف والخيرات وهو مردود بما ذكرناه، ثم قدم بعد ذلك شخص ولاه السلطان أمر الأوقاف فطلب أن يحدث على أراضي الأوقاف خراجاً متمسكاً بأن الخراج واجب في أرض الوقف وهو مردود عليه بما نقلناه عن المحقق ابن الهمام من أن الخراج ارتفع عن أراضي مصر إنما المأخوذ منها أجرة فصارت الأراضي بمنزلة دور السكنى لعدم من يجب عليه الخراج فإذا اشتراها إنسان من الإمام بشرطه شراء صحيحاً ملكها ولا خراج عليها فلا يجب عليه الخراج لأن الإمام قد أخذ البدل للمسلمين فإذا وقفها وقفها سالمة من المؤن فلا يجب الخراج فيها وتمامه فيما كتبناه في تلك السنة المسمى بالتحفة المرضية في الأراضي المصرية اهـ.
In Fath al-Qadir: What is taken now from Egyptian lands is only a rental payment instead of kharaj. Do you not see that the lands are not owned by the farmers? This is after we said that the land of Egypt is kharaji, and Allah knows best, as if due to the gradual death of the owners without leaving heirs, so it became for Bayt al-Mal. On this basis, it is not valid for the Imam to buy or sell any of it from the agent of Bayt al-Mal, because his oversight over the wealth of Muslims is like his oversight over the wealth of an orphan; he is not allowed to sell his real estate except due to necessity of not having anything else to spend. For this reason, I wrote in a fatwa that was raised to me regarding the purchase by Sultan Ashraf Barsbay of land from the one whom he appointed to supervise Bayt al-Mal: is it permissible for him to buy it from the one he appointed? So I wrote: If there is a need for the Muslims, and may Allah protect us, it is permissible. End. It is as if he answered that it is not permissible, as is clear, and this is based on the statement of the early scholars. As for the later scholars whose fatwa is relied upon, the permissibility of selling an orphan's real estate is not limited to what was mentioned; rather, it is also permitted when the deceased has a debt that cannot be paid except from it, or if someone wants to buy it at double its value. Similarly, we say that the Imam may sell real estate without necessity if someone wants it at double its value according to the relied-upon fatwa. This is an important matter about which a dispute occurred in our time in an inspection made by the deputy of Egypt on the rizq in the year 958 AH, until some claimed that the sales of lands from Bayt al-Mal are invalid, in order to use that to nullify awqaf and charitable endowments. But this is refuted by what we have mentioned. Then after that, a person was appointed by the Sultan for the affairs of awqaf, and he demanded that a kharaj be levied on waqf lands, arguing that kharaj is obligatory on waqf land. But this is refuted by what we have narrated from the investigator Ibn al-Humam: that kharaj has been lifted from Egyptian lands; what is taken from them is only rent. So the lands have become like residential houses, due to the absence of anyone upon whom kharaj is obligatory. If a person buys such land from the Imam with his condition for a valid purchase, he owns it, and there is no kharaj on it; it is not obligatory on the purchaser because the Imam has taken the compensation for the Muslims. If he then endows it, he endows it free from charges, so kharaj is not due on it. The full account is in what we wrote that year, called al-Tuhfa al-Mardiyya on Egyptian lands. End.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the status of Egyptian lands in the author's time. The author, Ibn Nujaym, quotes from Fath al-Qadir (by Ibn al-Humam) that what is currently taken from Egyptian lands is not actually kharaj but a rental payment (badal ijara). This is because the original owners have gradually died out without leaving heirs, so the lands have become property of the Bayt al-Mal (state treasury). The author then discusses the implications: the Imam's authority over Bayt al-Mal property is like that of a guardian over an orphan's property; selling such real estate is generally not permissible except in case of necessity (e.g., no other funds to meet needs). He relates a fatwa regarding Sultan Ashraf Barsbay's purchase of land from the very person he had appointed as overseer of Bayt al-Mal. The author's initial response was that it is permissible only if there is a general need. But he notes that according to later scholars, whose fatwa is relied upon (mufta bihi), the permissibility of selling an orphan's real estate is broader: it is allowed when the deceased has a debt to be paid, or if someone offers double the value. Thus, the Imam may also sell Bayt al-Mal lands without necessity if offered double value. The author then recounts a historical dispute in Egypt in the year 958 AH: an inspection led some to claim that land sales from Bayt al-Mal are invalid, aiming to nullify awqaf. He refutes this. Subsequently, an official tried to impose kharaj on waqf lands, but the author refutes this by citing Ibn al-Humam: kharaj has ceased on Egyptian lands; what is taken is rent. Therefore, lands bought from Bayt al-Mal are free of kharaj, and if endowed, no kharaj is due. The author refers to his own treatise, al-Tuhfa al-Mardiyya, for full detail. This passage thus clarifies the operative ruling for Egyptian lands in the later Hanafi fatwa tradition.

Key Terms
بدل إجارة (badal ijāra): A rental payment taken by the Imam from cultivators of Bayt al-Mal lands, standing in place of kharaj.
بيت المال (bayt al-māl): The state treasury; lands without owners become its property.
ضعف قيمته (diʿf qīmatih): Double the value; a condition for permissible sale of Bayt al-Mal land without necessity according to later scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Egyptian lands become rental rather than kharaj?
A) The Sultan abolished kharaj by decree.
B) The original owners gradually died without heirs, so lands reverted to Bayt al-Mal.
C) The land was not fertile enough for kharaj.
D) The farmers refused to pay kharaj.

Key Takeaway
Egyptian lands are now Bayt al-Mal property, and the payment collected is rental, not kharaj, due to the extinction of original owners.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Permissibility of Selling Bayt al-Mal Lands: Early vs Later Hanafi Positions
Discussion of the permissibility of selling Bayt al-Mal lands with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the early and later Hanafi positions on selling Bayt al-Mal (orphan) real estate, and state the mufta bihi condition (double value).

Core Idea
Later scholars permit sale of Bayt al-Mal land for double value even without necessity; early scholars restricted to dire need.

This passage not only clarifies the status of Egyptian lands but also resolves a practical fiqh question: under what conditions can the Imam sell land belonging to Bayt al-Mal? Ibn Nujaym contrasts the early position (mutaqaddimin) with the later position (mutakhkhirin) and establishes the operative fatwa, which has implications for land markets and endowments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the early position
Early scholars (mutaqaddimin) analogized Bayt al-Mal to orphan's property: the guardian (Imam) cannot sell real estate except in dire necessity (ضرورة). Note the phrase 'فلا يجوز له عقاره إلا بيع من ضرورة'.
This is the default cautious approach.
2
Identify the later position
Later scholars (mutakhkhirin) expanded the grounds: sale is also permitted if the deceased has a debt that cannot be paid otherwise, or if someone offers double the value (ضعف قيمته). The text says 'لا ينحصر جواز بيع عقار اليتيم فيما ذكر بل فيه وفيما إذا كان على الميت دين ... أو رغب فيه بضعف قيمته'.
Double value is a proxy for benefit to Bayt al-Mal.
3
Understand the tarjih
The mufta bihi is the later position. The author explicitly says 'على المفتى به' after mentioning double value. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Tarjih authority: Ibn Nujaym and Ibn al-Humam.
4
Apply to the historical dispute
In 958 AH, some claimed land sales from Bayt al-Mal were invalid. Ibn Nujaym refutes them by citing the later position: such sales are valid, especially when double value was offered. This protects awqaf and charitable endowments.
The dispute had a concrete political context.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Sale of Land from Bayt al-Mal and Fall of Kharaj
Fatwa and commentary on Imam's sale of public land and removal of kharaj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the Imam's sale of land from the public treasury and explain the status of kharaj on such land according to later Hanafi scholars.

Core Idea
The Imam may sell public land for double its value; land then becomes free of kharaj, replaced by rent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles two pressing issues in Ottoman Egypt: the ruler's authority to sell land from the public treasury (bayt al-mal) and whether such land remains subject to kharaj (land tax). The author contrasts early Hanafi strictness with the later relied-upon view, and refutes attempts to invalidate sales or impose kharaj on endowments. Understanding this ruling is crucial for grasping how Hanafi fiqh adapts to changing administrative needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage states that according to later scholars (mutakhkhirin), whose opinion is mufta bihi, the Imam may sell land from bayt al-mal even without urgent need, provided the buyer pays double its value (ضعف القيمة). Note this condition—it is the key to permissibility.
Earlier scholars (mutqaddimun) only allowed sale in case of dire necessity, e.g., to pay debts of the deceased.
2
Analyze the historical dispute
The text mentions a controversy in 958 AH where some claimed such sales were invalid to undermine endowments. The author refutes this, asserting the validity of sales conducted by the Imam under the later view.
This shows how legal rulings can be weaponized in political disputes; the Hanafi response protects public interest.
3
Apply the ruling on kharaj
Ibn al-Humam's view is cited: kharaj has been lifted from Egyptian lands—what is collected is rent (ujra), not tax. Hence, land bought from the Imam is owned free of kharaj, and if made waqf, it remains free.
The abolition of kharaj is based on the absence of anyone obligated to pay it (e.g., non-Muslim conquerors).
4
Recognize the practical outcome
The sale transfers full ownership without future tax liability for the buyer, and the Imam has already compensated the treasury (bayt al-mal) by receiving the price.
This facilitates land transactions and protects endowments from additional burdens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير باب العشر والخراج والجزية
١٧٩
لضرورة عدم وجود ما ينفقه سواه فلذا كتبت في فتوى رفعت إلي في شراء السلطان الأشرف برسباي الأرض ممن ولاه نظر بيت المال هل يجوز شراؤه منه وهو الذي ولاه؟ فكتبت : إذا كان بالمسلمين حاجة والعياذ بالله تعالى جاز ذلك اهـ . كأنه أجاب لا يجوز كما لا يخفى وهو مبني على قول المتقدمين، أما على قول المتأخرين المفتى به لا ينحصر جواز بيع عقار اليتيم فيما ذكر بل فيه وفيما إذا كان على الميت دين لا وفاء له إلا منه أو رغب فيه بضعف قيمته فكذلك نقول للإمام بيع العقار لغير حاجة إذا رغب فيه بضعف قيمته على المفتى به، وهذه مسألة مهمة وقع النزاع فيها في زماننا في تفتيش وقع من نائب مصر على الرزق في سنة ثمان وخمسين وتسعمائة حتى ادعى بعضهم بأن المبايعات للأراضي من بيت المال غير صحيحة ليتوصل بذلك إلى إبطال الأوقاف والخيرات وهو مردود بما ذكرناه، ثم قدم بعد ذلك شخص ولاه السلطان أمر الأوقاف فطلب أن يحدث على أراضي الأوقاف خراجاً متمسكاً بأن الخراج واجب في أرض الوقف وهو مردود عليه بما نقلناه عن المحقق ابن الهمام من أن الخراج ارتفع عن أراضي مصر إنما المأخوذ منها أجرة فصارت الأراضي بمنزلة دور السكنى لعدم من يجب عليه الخراج فإذا اشتراها إنسان من الإمام بشرطه شراء صحيحاً ملكها ولا خراج عليها فلا يجب عليه الخراج لأن الإمام قد أخذ البدل للمسلمين فإذا وقفها وقفها سالمة من المؤن فلا يجب الخراج فيها وتمامه فيما كتبناه في تلك السنة المسمى بالتحفة المرضية في الأراضي المصرية اهـ.
The Book of Siyar, Chapter of Ushr, Kharaj, and Jizya. 179. Due to the necessity of not having anything else to spend, I wrote in a fatwa that was presented to me regarding the purchase by Sultan al-Ashraf Barsbay of land from someone whom he had appointed as inspector of the public treasury: Is it permissible for him to buy from him while he is his appointee? I wrote: If there is a need among the Muslims, and Allah forbid otherwise, then it is permissible. End. It is as if he answered that it is not permissible, as is not hidden, and this is based on the statement of the earlier scholars. As for the later scholars, who are the ones given fatwa, the permissibility of selling the orphan's real estate is not limited to what was mentioned, but also includes if the deceased had a debt that cannot be paid except from it, or if there is a desire for it at double its value. So similarly we say for the Imam, the sale of real estate without need is permissible if there is a desire for it at double its value, according to the relied-upon position. This is an important issue, and a dispute occurred in our time regarding an inspection made by the deputy of Egypt on the livelihood in the year 958 AH, until some claimed that the sales of lands from the public treasury are invalid, thereby seeking to nullify endowments and charitable deeds. This is refuted by what we have mentioned. Then after that, a person appointed by the Sultan over endowments came and demanded to impose kharaj on endowed lands, arguing that kharaj is obligatory on waqf land. This is refuted by what we have transmitted from the muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam: that kharaj has been lifted from the lands of Egypt; indeed, what is taken from them is rent, so the lands have become like residential houses due to the absence of anyone upon whom kharaj is obligatory. So if a person buys it from the Imam with his condition, a valid purchase, he owns it and there is no kharaj upon it; thus kharaj is not obligatory on him because the Imam has taken the compensation for the Muslims. If he then endows it, he endows it free from charges, so kharaj is not obligatory on it. The completion of this is in what we wrote in that year, named al-Tuhfa al-Mardiyya fi al-Ardi al-Misriyya. End.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a fatwa and commentary on the permissibility of the Imam selling land from the public treasury (bayt al-mal), and the status of kharaj on Egyptian lands. The author recounts a fatwa request regarding Sultan Barsbay buying land from an official he appointed. Initially, he opined that it is permissible if there is dire need, but notes that the earlier scholars (mutqaddimun) did not permit such sales except in specific cases, while the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) whose view is mufta bihi allow the Imam to sell land for double its value even without need. The author refutes claims that such sales are invalid, and also refutes the attempt to impose kharaj on waqf lands, citing Ibn al-Humam's view that kharaj has been lifted from Egyptian lands; what is collected is rent (ujra), not kharaj. Therefore, land bought from the Imam is free of kharaj, and if it is made into waqf, it remains free. The discussion highlights the distinction between early and later Hanafi positions, and emphasizes the authority of the Imam to manage public property.

Key Terms
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslims, managed by the Imam or his deputy.
خراج (kharaj): Land tax imposed on kharaji lands; according to the text, it has been replaced by rent on Egyptian lands.
المفتى به (al-mufta bihi): The relied-upon position for issuing fatwas, often the view of later Hanafi scholars.
ضعف القيمة (di'f al-qima): Double the value; the condition for selling public land without necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirin), under what condition may the Imam sell land from bayt al-mal without a pressing need?
A) Only if the land is non-agricultural
B) If the buyer pays double the value
C) Only if the buyer is a Muslim
D) Only if the sale is approved by a council of scholars

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi position allows the Imam to sell public land for double its value, and such land is free of kharaj; this protects endowments and public interest.

Three Cases Where Kharaj Remains Due
Commentary on the ruling that kharaj is due in three specific cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where kharaj is due on kharaj land and explain the rationale and limitation for each.

Core Idea
Neglect, conversion, or purchase do not erase kharaj—the land's tax persists.

Kharaj is a perpetual land tax on conquered territories. The Hanafi school rules that this obligation remains undiminished even when the owner stops cultivating, embraces Islam, or sells to a Muslim. However, this theoretical ruling is rarely given as a formal fatwa to prevent abuse by oppressive rulers. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each case and the practical nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
List the three situations mentioned in the passage where kharaj remains due: owner neglects cultivation, owner converts to Islam, or a Muslim buys kharaj land from a dhimmi.
2
Understand the rationale for each
For neglect: the owner had ability and caused the loss. For conversion: kharaj is a maintenance burden on the land, not a religious penalty. For purchase: the Companions' practice shows Muslims can own such land and pay kharaj.
3
Note the condition of fixed kharaj
The ruling applies only to fixed (mawzuf) kharaj, not proportional (muqasama) kharaj, because proportional kharaj is tied to actual produce.
This is a crucial distinction.
4
Recognize the fatwa limitation
Although the ruling is legally sound, it is not given as fatwa (la yufra bihi) to prevent oppressors from exploiting it to demand higher taxes unjustly.
This highlights the Hanafi concern for ma'aliq (consequences).
5
Consider the owner's inability
If the owner is genuinely unable to cultivate due to poverty, the Imam has options like sharecropping, leasing, or sale, but there is a dispute (Abu Yusuf prefers a loan from treasury).

Source Text

If its owner neglects it, or becomes Muslim, or a Muslim buys kharaj land, the kharaj is due. And there is no tithe on the produce of kharaj land. Some of the subjects' crop [if a pest?] his guardian [spends] what he spent on cultivation from their treasury, and the merchant said: the partner shares in loss as he shares in profit. So if the Imam does not give him anything, at least he should not make him liable for the kharaj. End. He said: (And if its owner neglects it, or becomes Muslim, or a Muslim buys kharaj land, the kharaj is due) i.e., the kharaj. As for the first [case], because ability was established and he himself caused its loss. They said: one who transfers to the better of two options without an excuse owes the higher kharaj, because he is the one who wasted the increase; as if the land were suitable for saffron but he planted barley. This is known but not given as a fatwa, lest oppressors become bold in taking people's wealth, because if we gave fatwa accordingly, every oppressor would claim for land not of that nature that it used to grow saffron, taking its kharaj, resulting in oppression and injustice. It is conditioned that he is the one who neglects, because if someone prevented him from cultivation, he does not owe kharaj due to lack of ability. And it is conditioned that the kharaj is fixed, because his statement is about it; for if it were proportional kharaj, nothing is owed by neglect; thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. And by attributing the neglect to him, he indicates that he was able to cultivate but did not. So if the owner is unable to cultivate due to lack of strength and its means, the Imam may give it to another for sharecropping, taking the kharaj from the owner's share and keeping the rest for the owner; or if he wishes, he may lease it and take the kharaj from the rent; or if he wishes, he may cultivate it at the treasury's expense; if he is unable to do that and finds no one to accept it, he may sell it and take the kharaj from its price. This is without dispute. And from Abu Yusuf: he gives the incapacitated person his sufficiency from the treasury as a loan. And in Jam' al-Shahid: he sold a kharaj land, if there remains of the year an amount [sufficient for cultivation?], locusts that cannot be repelled, and that it drops the kharaj by its eating; and there is no doubt that the worm and mouse are analogous to locusts in inability to repel. And similar to what is in al-Bazzaziyya, Mulla Miskin stated, and in al-Nahr after quoting his saying 'From it is known etc.', I say: regarding the worm not being a celestial calamity, there is apparent consideration; rather, one should not hesitate about it being celestial and that one cannot guard against it... until the end of his speech. And I say: if it is abundant and prevailing and cannot be repelled by a trick, the kharaj should drop; if it can be repelled, it does not drop; this is the correct course. He said: (And it is conditioned that the kharaj is fixed, because his statement is about it, etc.) Al-Ramli said: likewise, if the produce is destroyed in proportional kharaj before or after harvest, nothing is due from him because it is attached to the produce in reality, and its ruling is the ruling of a partner in joint ownership; he is not liable except by transgression. Know that, for it is important and occurs frequently in our lands. In al-Khaniyya, there is an explicit statement that it drops after harvest regarding the landowner and is obligatory on the share of the cultivator, the reason being that the land in his share is like rented property. In al-Walwalijiyya, there is something contradicting it, and what is in al-Khaniyya is stronger in source and clearer in aspect, so it should be relied upon. He said: (If the owner is unable to cultivate, etc.) Al-Ramli said: then if his ability returns, the Imam takes it back from the one who has it and returns it to its owner, except specifically in the case of sale; as stated in al-Tatar. from al-Dhakhira. He said: (And in Jam' al-Shahid he sold something portion of land of Egypt) The Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, that if the buyer is able to cultivate, the kharaj is on him; otherwise on the seller; thus in al-Binaya. And we have already stated that now it is not kharaj land, but rather by lease, so nothing is on the peasant if he neglects it, and he is not compelled because of it. From this it is known that some farmers who leave cultivation and reside in [the city] have nothing against them. What oppressors do in harming them is forbidden, especially if he wants to occupy himself in Egypt with Quran and knowledge, like the neighbors of al-Azhar Mosque. As for the second [case], that the one who becomes Muslim from the people of kharaj, the kharaj is taken from him as before, because there is a meaning of maintenance in it, so maintenance is considered in the state of continuity, so it is possible to maintain it on the Muslim. As for the third [case], which is when a Muslim buys kharaj land from a dhimmi, as we have said, and indeed the Companions bought kharaj lands and used to pay their kharaj, indicating the permissibility of purchase and taking the kharaj and paying it for the Muslim without dislike.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the obligation of kharaj on kharaj land in three specific situations: when the owner neglects cultivation, when the owner becomes Muslim, and when a Muslim purchases the land from a dhimmi. The author begins by clarifying that the ruling applies to fixed (mawzuf) kharaj, not proportional (muqasama) kharaj, because for proportional kharaj, if the owner neglects, nothing is owed since kharaj is linked to actual produce. For the first case of neglect, the key point is that the owner had the ability to cultivate but caused its loss; thus, the kharaj remains due. However, the author adds a crucial qualification: this ruling is known but not given as a fatwa (la yufra bihi) because it would be used by oppressors to unjustly claim higher kharaj. This is a common Hanafi principle: the theoretical ruling is correct, but its application is suspended due to widespread corruption. The commentary then discusses scenarios when the owner is genuinely unable to cultivate due to poverty: the Imam has several options, including sharecropping, leasing, or even selling the land to collect the kharaj. A difference from Imam Abu Yusuf is noted: he prefers the Imam to give the owner a loan from the treasury. The passage also addresses the effect of pests (locusts, worms, mice) that destroy the crop: if the pest is unavoidable and overwhelming, the kharaj is dropped; otherwise not. This is analogized to celestial calamities. The discussion then clarifies a dispute within the Hanafi school about proportional kharaj when produce is destroyed after harvest: the stronger view (from al-Khaniyya) is that the landowner is free but the sharecropper owes his share. Finally, the second and third cases are explained: conversion to Islam does not remove kharaj because it has the meaning of a maintenance burden on the land, and the Companions' practice confirms that Muslims can own and pay kharaj on such lands. The commentary provides a comprehensive view of the subtleties in applying kharaj obligations.

Key Terms
خراج (kharāj): A land tax imposed on conquered lands that remain in the hands of non-Muslims or are owned by Muslims subject to kharaj.
عشر (‘ushr): A tithe (10% or 5%) on produce from lands that came into Muslim hands through peaceful submission.
خراج موظف (kharāj mawẓūf): Fixed kharaj – a predetermined annual amount on the land regardless of whether it is cultivated.
خراج مقاسمة (kharāj muqāsama): Proportional kharaj – a percentage of the actual produce (e.g., half or one-third).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the ruling on the three cases not given as a fatwa?
A) The ruling is weak in evidence.
B) Oppressors might misuse it to claim higher kharaj unjustly.
C) It contradicts earlier authorities.
D) It only applies to non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Kharaj is a land-based obligation that endures through changes in owner's religion, neglect, or sale, but fatwa is withheld to prevent injustice.

Fixed vs Proportional Kharaj: Does It Repeat?
Ruling statement on kharaj repetition types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fixed (mawẓūf) and proportional (muqāsama) kharaj and state whether each repeats within a year.

Core Idea
Fixed kharaj is annual; proportional kharaj repeats with each harvest.

The first subsidiary ruling clarifies the nature of kharaj: if it is a fixed amount, it is due once per year regardless of multiple harvests; if it is a share of the crop, it is due with each harvest. This distinction affects the liability of the landowner and the calculation of dues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of kharaj
Read the phrase 'if it is fixed' (موظف) and 'proportional' (مقاسمة). Understand that the criteria is whether the amount is a lump sum or a fraction of produce.
Fixed = mawẓūf; proportional = muqāsama.
2
Link attachment to repetition
Fixed kharaj is attached to the land itself, so it does not depend on the number of harvests. Proportional kharaj is attached to the actual produce, so it repeats with each crop.
The text says 'because it is attached to the produce in reality, like the tithe.'
3
Apply the rule
Given a scenario: land yields two harvests in one year. If kharaj is fixed, only one payment is due. If proportional, two payments (one per harvest) are due.
This is a direct application of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع : لا يتكرر الخراج بتكرر الخارج في سنة إذا كان موظفاً وإن كان خراج مقاسمة تكرر لتعلقه بالخارج حقيقة كالعشر، ولو وهب السلطان لإنسان خراج أرضه ليس له أن يقبل وإن كان مصرفاً له أن يقبل . ولو ترك السلطان لإنسان خراج أرضه جاز عند أبي يوسف. اختلفوا : أرضاً خراجية الخ) قال في التتارخانية هذه المسألة على وجهين : الأول أن تكون الأرض فارغة والجواب فيه أنه إن بقي من السنة مقدار ما يقدر المشتري على زراعتها قبل دخول السنة الثانية فالخراج على المشتري وإلا فعلى البائع ثم اختلف المشايخ أن المعتبر زرع الحنطة أو الشعير أو أي زرع كان فالفقيه أبو نصر يعتبر أي زرع كان والفقيه أبو القاسم يعتبر زرع الحنطة أو الشعير. وكذلك أنه هل يشترط إدراك الريع بكماله؟ وفي واقعات الناطفي الفتوى على أنه مقدر بثلاثة أشهر إن بقيت يجب على المشتري وإلا فعلى البائع وهذا منه اعتبار زرع الدخن وإدراك الريع فإن ريع الدخن يدرك في مثل هذه المدة. الوجه الثاني إذا كانت الأرض مزروعة، فإن كان الزرع لم يبلغ بعد فباعها مع الزرع فالخراج على المشتري على كل حال، وإن كان الزرع قد بلغ وانعقد الحب فإن هذا وما لو باع أرضاً فارغة في الحكم سواء. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد: رجل له أرض خراج باعها من رجل ومكثت عند المشتري شهراً ثم باعها المشتري من رجل آخر ومكثت عنده شهراً أيضاً
Subsidiary issues: Kharaj does not repeat with the repetition of produce in one year if it is fixed; if it is proportional kharaj, it repeats because it is attached to the produce in reality, like the tithe. If the sultan gifts to a person the kharaj of his land, he may not accept it; if it is [the sultan's] expenditure [from the kharaj], he may accept. If the sultan leaves [i.e., forgives] for a person the kharaj of his land, it is permissible according to Abu Yusuf. They differed [regarding] a kharaj land...), he said in al-Tatar. this matter has two aspects: first, that the land is empty; the answer is that if there remains of the year an amount sufficient for the buyer to cultivate before the entry of the second year, the kharaj is on the buyer; otherwise on the seller. Then the scholars differed: what is the standard? Is it the cultivation of wheat or barley or any crop? Al-Faqih Abu Nasr considers any crop, and al-Faqih Abu al-Qasim considers wheat or barley. Also, is it a condition to realize the full yield? In Waqi'at al-Natifi, the fatwa is that it is estimated as three months: if that much time remains, the kharaj is on the buyer; otherwise on the seller. This is based on considering the cultivation of millet and the realization of its yield, for millet yield is realized in such a period. The second aspect: if the land is already planted, and the crop has not yet ripened, and he sells it with the crop, the kharaj is on the buyer in any case. If the crop has already ripened and the grain has hardened, then this is like selling empty land in terms of ruling. And in Nawadir Ibn Sami'a from Muhammad: a man had a kharaj land, sold it to a man, and it stayed with the buyer for a month; then the buyer sold it to another man, and it stayed with him for a month also.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage provides subsidiary rulings (furu') related to kharaj. First, fixed kharaj (mawzuf) does not repeat within a year if the land produces multiple harvests; only the annual amount is due. However, proportional kharaj (muqasama) repeats with each harvest because it is a share of the crop, like 'ushr. Second, regarding gifting or forgiving kharaj: if the ruler (sultan) gives a person the kharaj amount from his own land, the person cannot accept it because it is a treasury right; but if the ruler grants it as a discretionary expenditure (masraf), it is permissible. Imam Abu Yusuf allows the ruler to simply drop the kharaj for a person. Third, the passage discusses in detail the sale of kharaj land and who bears the kharaj. The general rule is that the kharaj for the year is on the seller unless sufficient time remains for the buyer to cultivate and realize a crop before the next year's kharaj assessment. The scholars differ on what is considered 'sufficient time': some say any crop, others only wheat or barley; the fatwa in Waqi'at al-Natifi sets a specific period of three months, analogous to the growth of millet. If the land is sold with an existing crop, the buyer bears the kharaj if the crop is unripe; if ripe and grains hardened, it is like empty land (so based on time remaining). A narrated case from Muhammad al-Shaybani illustrates multiple sales within a year, implying that liability passes to each buyer proportionally based on possession duration, though the text is cut off here.

Key Terms
موظف (mawẓūf): Fixed annual kharaj amount, not dependent on the number of harvests.
مقاسمة (muqāsama): Proportional kharaj: a share of the actual produce (e.g., half, third).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of kharaj repeats within a year?
A) Fixed (mawẓūf) kharaj
B) Proportional (muqāsama) kharaj
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Fixed kharaj is annual; proportional kharaj repeats with each harvest.

Can the Ruler Gift or Forgive Kharaj?
Ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on ruler's power over kharaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule on a ruler gifting kharaj to an individual and identify the intra-Hanafi difference on forgiveness (tark).

Core Idea
A ruler cannot gift kharaj to an individual, but can forgive it according to Abu Yusuf.

The second issue concerns the ruler's authority over kharaj revenue. Since kharaj is a public treasury (bayt al-mal) right, the ruler cannot gift it to a specific person. However, he can forgive it (drop the debt) according to Imam Abu Yusuf, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani do not allow it because it is effectively a gift of public funds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of kharaj
Kharaj is a right of the bayt al-mal (public treasury). The ruler is a trustee, not an owner. He cannot give away treasury property.
This is the basis for the prohibition of gifting.
2
Differentiate between gift (hiba) and forgiveness (tark)
Gifting transfers ownership to the individual; forgiveness is waiving the debt. Abu Yusuf considers forgiveness as a drop of claim, not a transfer, hence permissible.
The text uses 'wahaba' (gift) and 'taraka' (left/forgave).
3
Note the exception for masraf (expenditure)
If the ruler assigns the kharaj as a discretionary expenditure from the treasury (masraf), the recipient can accept it because it is a legitimate disbursement.
The text says: 'if it is [the sultan's] expenditure, he may accept.'
4
Identify the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf allows forgiveness; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not. The mufta bihi is the majority view (prohibition of forgiveness).
The text explicitly states 'according to Abu Yusuf', implying difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع : لا يتكرر الخراج بتكرر الخارج في سنة إذا كان موظفاً وإن كان خراج مقاسمة تكرر لتعلقه بالخارج حقيقة كالعشر، ولو وهب السلطان لإنسان خراج أرضه ليس له أن يقبل وإن كان مصرفاً له أن يقبل . ولو ترك السلطان لإنسان خراج أرضه جاز عند أبي يوسف. اختلفوا : أرضاً خراجية الخ) قال في التتارخانية هذه المسألة على وجهين : الأول أن تكون الأرض فارغة والجواب فيه أنه إن بقي من السنة مقدار ما يقدر المشتري على زراعتها قبل دخول السنة الثانية فالخراج على المشتري وإلا فعلى البائع ثم اختلف المشايخ أن المعتبر زرع الحنطة أو الشعير أو أي زرع كان فالفقيه أبو نصر يعتبر أي زرع كان والفقيه أبو القاسم يعتبر زرع الحنطة أو الشعير. وكذلك أنه هل يشترط إدراك الريع بكماله؟ وفي واقعات الناطفي الفتوى على أنه مقدر بثلاثة أشهر إن بقيت يجب على المشتري وإلا فعلى البائع وهذا منه اعتبار زرع الدخن وإدراك الريع فإن ريع الدخن يدرك في مثل هذه المدة. الوجه الثاني إذا كانت الأرض مزروعة، فإن كان الزرع لم يبلغ بعد فباعها مع الزرع فالخراج على المشتري على كل حال، وإن كان الزرع قد بلغ وانعقد الحب فإن هذا وما لو باع أرضاً فارغة في الحكم سواء. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد: رجل له أرض خراج باعها من رجل ومكثت عند المشتري شهراً ثم باعها المشتري من رجل آخر ومكثت عنده شهراً أيضاً
Subsidiary issues: Kharaj does not repeat with the repetition of produce in one year if it is fixed; if it is proportional kharaj, it repeats because it is attached to the produce in reality, like the tithe. If the sultan gifts to a person the kharaj of his land, he may not accept it; if it is [the sultan's] expenditure [from the kharaj], he may accept. If the sultan leaves [i.e., forgives] for a person the kharaj of his land, it is permissible according to Abu Yusuf. They differed [regarding] a kharaj land...), he said in al-Tatar. this matter has two aspects: first, that the land is empty; the answer is that if there remains of the year an amount sufficient for the buyer to cultivate before the entry of the second year, the kharaj is on the buyer; otherwise on the seller. Then the scholars differed: what is the standard? Is it the cultivation of wheat or barley or any crop? Al-Faqih Abu Nasr considers any crop, and al-Faqih Abu al-Qasim considers wheat or barley. Also, is it a condition to realize the full yield? In Waqi'at al-Natifi, the fatwa is that it is estimated as three months: if that much time remains, the kharaj is on the buyer; otherwise on the seller. This is based on considering the cultivation of millet and the realization of its yield, for millet yield is realized in such a period. The second aspect: if the land is already planted, and the crop has not yet ripened, and he sells it with the crop, the kharaj is on the buyer in any case. If the crop has already ripened and the grain has hardened, then this is like selling empty land in terms of ruling. And in Nawadir Ibn Sami'a from Muhammad: a man had a kharaj land, sold it to a man, and it stayed with the buyer for a month; then the buyer sold it to another man, and it stayed with him for a month also.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage provides subsidiary rulings (furu') related to kharaj. First, fixed kharaj (mawzuf) does not repeat within a year if the land produces multiple harvests; only the annual amount is due. However, proportional kharaj (muqasama) repeats with each harvest because it is a share of the crop, like 'ushr. Second, regarding gifting or forgiving kharaj: if the ruler (sultan) gives a person the kharaj amount from his own land, the person cannot accept it because it is a treasury right; but if the ruler grants it as a discretionary expenditure (masraf), it is permissible. Imam Abu Yusuf allows the ruler to simply drop the kharaj for a person. Third, the passage discusses in detail the sale of kharaj land and who bears the kharaj. The general rule is that the kharaj for the year is on the seller unless sufficient time remains for the buyer to cultivate and realize a crop before the next year's kharaj assessment. The scholars differ on what is considered 'sufficient time': some say any crop, others only wheat or barley; the fatwa in Waqi'at al-Natifi sets a specific period of three months, analogous to the growth of millet. If the land is sold with an existing crop, the buyer bears the kharaj if the crop is unripe; if ripe and grains hardened, it is like empty land (so based on time remaining). A narrated case from Muhammad al-Shaybani illustrates multiple sales within a year, implying that liability passes to each buyer proportionally based on possession duration, though the text is cut off here.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): Gift; transferring ownership without compensation.
ترك (tark): Forgiveness; dropping the claim to kharaj.
مصرف (maṣraf): Expenditure; a legitimate disbursement from the treasury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can a person accept kharaj from the sultan?
A) When the sultan gifts it
B) When the sultan forgives it
C) When it is designated as expenditure (masraf)
D) Never

Key Takeaway
A ruler cannot gift kharaj, but can assign it as expenditure; forgiveness is allowed only by Abu Yusuf, but the majority prohibits it.

Sale of Kharaj Land: Who Bears the Annual Kharaj?
Expansion probe on sale of kharaj land with detailed conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for who is liable for kharaj when kharaj land is sold, distinguishing between empty land and land with crop, and using the three-month benchmark.

Core Idea
The buyer bears kharaj only if sufficient time remains for cultivation; the fatwa sets three months as the benchmark.

The extensive third issue deals with the sale of kharaj land and allocation of the annual kharaj. The general rule is that the seller pays unless the buyer has enough time to cultivate and realize a crop before the next year's kharaj assessment. Scholars differed on the criteria for 'enough time', and the fatwa in Waqi'at al-Natifi settled on three months. Additional rules apply when the land is sold with an existing crop.

Guided Path
1
Classify the land condition: empty or planted
Determine whether the land is empty (not cultivated) or planted. If planted, note the stage of the crop (unripe or ripe).
The passage divides the discussion into two aspects (wajhayn).
2
Apply the time sufficiency rule for empty land
If the land is empty, check if the time remaining in the year is enough for the buyer to cultivate and reap before the next year. The benchmark is three months, based on millet growth.
The fatwa: three months. If more than three months remain, buyer pays; otherwise seller pays.
3
Consider the ikhtilaf on the standard crop
Some scholars consider any crop (Abu Nasr), others only wheat or barley (Abu al-Qasim). The fatwa uses three months implicitly covering millet, so it's a practical standard.
This ikhtilaf affects when time starts; but the fatwa simplifies.
4
Determine ruling for planted land
If the land is planted with unripe crop (lam yablugh), the buyer pays unconditionally. If the crop is ripe (grain hardened), the land is treated like empty land and the three-month rule applies.
The text: 'if the crop has not yet ripened... kharaj on buyer in any case; if it has ripened... same as empty land.'

Source Text

Property of the Apostate: Suspended Ownership
Ruling on whether the apostate's property ownership ceases or remains suspended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the apostate's property ownership and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding its status and practical consequences.

Core Idea
An apostate's property is neither fully forfeited nor fully retained — it is suspended pending his return to Islam or death.

What happens to a person's property when they leave Islam? Does it become state property, remain theirs, or hang in limbo? The Hanafi school has a unique answer: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the ownership ceases in a suspended manner, while his two chief disciples hold that full ownership continues until death. This lesson unpacks that intra-madhhab dispute and its real-world effects on gifts, sales, leases, and wills.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is apostasy (ridda) affecting property. According to the matn, the ruling is 'suspended cessation' (zawal mawquf) of ownership. Determine what 'suspended' means in legal terms.
This is the foundation: ownership is not gone but not fully present.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas: the apostate is compared to a harbi (enemy combatant) under Muslim control, whose property becomes booty. But the apostate is also called to Islam, so the effect is delayed.
The qiyas is khafi (hidden analogy) based on the illa of being a combatant with hope of return.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the analogy yield 'suspension'? Because the hukm of the asl (immediate seizure) is modified by the far's unique feature: the apostate may re-embrace Islam, so we wait.
This is why the cessation is 'mawquf' — the cause (ridda) is present but effect is postponed.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani reject suspension entirely. For them, ownership remains intact because the apostate remains a legally responsible person with needs until execution.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the Sahibayn's proof is the apostate's ongoing legal capacity.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that of the Sahibayn: ownership remains until death or joining Dar al-Harb. This is more practical because it avoids uncertainty in transactions.
Most later Hanafi texts follow this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويزول ملك المرتد عن ماله زوالاً موقوفاً فإن أسلم عاد ملكه وإن مات أو قتل على ردته ورث كسب إسلامه وارثه) قالوا: وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وعندهما لا يزول ملكه لأنه مكلف محتاج فإلى أن يقتل يبقى ملكه كالمحكوم عليه بالرجم والقصاص، وله أنه حربي مقهور تحت أيدينا حتى يقتل ولا يقتل إلا بالحراب وهذا يجب زوال ملكه ومالكيته غير أنه مدعو إلى الإسلام بالإجبار عليه ويرجى عوده إليه فتوقفنا في أمره؛ فإن أسلم جعل العارض كأن لم يكن في حق هذا الحكم فصار كأن لم يزل مسلماً ولم يعمل بالسبب، وإن مات أو قتل على ردته أو لحق بدار الحرب وحكم بلحاقه استقر أمره فعمل السبب عمله وزال ملكه. ثم اختلف الشيخان في حكم تبرعاته فقال أبو يوسف: من المال كتصرف من وجب عليه القصاص. وقال محمد هو بمنزلة المريض فتكون من الثلث لكونه على شرف التلف وفي البدائع: لا خلاف أنه إذا أسلم أن أمواله باقية على حكم ملكه وأنه إذا مات أو قتل أو لحق بدار الحرب أنها تزول عن ملكه، وإنما الخلاف في زوالها بهذه الأشياء الثلاثة مقصوراً على الحال وهو قولهما أو مستنداً إلى وقت وجود الردة وهو قوله وثمرته تظهر في تصرفاته فعندهما نافذة قبل الإسلام وعنده موقوفة لوقوف أملاكه اهـ. قيد بالملك لأنه لا توقف في إحباط طاعاته ووقوع الفرقة بينه وبين امرأته وتجديد الإيمان فإن الارتداد بالنسبة إليها قد عمل عمله؛ كذا في العناية. وذكر في الخانية إذا استأجر المسلم داراً أو عقاراً أو منقولاً ثم ارتد - والعياذ بالله تعالى ـ ولحق بدار الحرب وقضى القاضي بلحاقه تبطل إجارته كأنه مات وكذا إذا استأجر ثم ارتد. ولو أوصى لرجل بثلث ماله ثم ارتد ولحق بدار الحرب أو لم يلحق بطلت وصيته، وكذا لو أوصى إلى رجل وجعله قيماً في ماله ثم ارتد ولحق بدار الحرب أو لم يلحق بطل إيصاؤه. وإن وكل رجلاً ثم ارتد الموكل ولحق بدار الحرب ينعزل وكيله في قولهم، وإن عاد إلينا مسلماً هل يعود وكيلاً؟ ذكر في الوكالة أنه لا يعود، وذكر في السير أنه يعود، ولو ارتد الوكيل ولحق وقضى به ثم عاد
His statement: (And the property of the apostate ceases from his ownership in a suspended cessation; if he becomes Muslim, his ownership returns; if he dies or is killed in his apostasy, his heir inherits the acquisition of his Islam.) They said: This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (the two), his ownership does not cease because he is a legally responsible person with needs; until he is killed, his ownership remains like that of one sentenced to stoning or retaliation. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that he is a combatant subdued under our control until he is killed, and he is not killed except by weapons, and this necessitates that his ownership and being an owner cease, except that he is called to Islam by compulsion and his return is hoped for, so we suspend his matter. If he becomes Muslim, the obstacle is made as if it never existed in regard to this ruling, so he becomes as if he never ceased being Muslim, and the cause does not operate. If he dies or is killed in his apostasy, or joins Dar al-Harb and the judge rules on his joining, his matter becomes settled, and the cause operates and his ownership ceases. Then the two Imams differed on the ruling of his donations: Abu Yusuf said: from the wealth like the transaction of one who is obliged retaliation. Muhammad said: he is like a sick person, so it is from one-third because he is on the verge of destruction. In al-Bada'i: There is no dispute that if he becomes Muslim, his properties remain under his ownership, and that if he dies or is killed or joins Dar al-Harb, they cease from his ownership. The dispute is only about the cessation by these three things being limited to the present (their view) or based on the time of apostasy (his view). The fruit appears in his transactions: according to them, they are effective before Islam; according to him, they are suspended due to the suspension of his properties. End quote. He restricted it to ownership because there is no suspension in the nullification of his acts of worship, the occurrence of separation between him and his wife, and the renewal of faith, for apostasy with regard to them has already operated. So in al-'Inayah. It is mentioned in al-Khaniyya: If a Muslim rents a house or property or movable and then apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, and the judge rules on his joining, his lease is invalidated as if he died; likewise if he rents[?]. And if he bequeaths to a man one-third of his wealth and then apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb or does not join, his will is invalidated; likewise if he appoints a man as executor and makes him a guardian over his wealth, then apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb or does not join, his appointment is invalidated. And if he appoints a man as agent and then the principal apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, his agent is removed according to their statement; if he returns to us as a Muslim, does he return as agent? It is mentioned in Agency that he does not return, and in Siyar that he returns. And if the agent apostatizes, joins, and the judge rules accordingly, then returns...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the property of an apostate. The matn states that the apostate's property leaves his ownership in a suspended manner; if he returns to Islam, ownership returns; if he dies or is killed in apostasy, his heirs inherit the property he acquired during his Islam. The commentary explains that this is the position of Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), ownership does not cease at all because the apostate remains a legally responsible person in need until he is executed. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the apostate is essentially a combatant (harbi) under our control; his property should become war booty, but because we hope for his return to Islam, we suspend the effect. When he returns to Islam, the suspension is lifted; when he dies or joins Dar al-Harb, the cause fully operates and ownership ceases. The two Imams then differ on the validity of the apostate's voluntary transactions (gifts, bequests, etc.): Abu Yusuf compares it to a person under capital punishment (transactions valid from capital wealth), while Muhammad compares it to a terminally ill patient (transactions valid only from one-third of wealth). The relied-upon view (al-mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn, i.e., ownership remains until death. The text then discusses specific applications: leases are invalidated if he joins Dar al-Harb; wills and executorships are invalidated; agency is terminated but with dispute on revival if he returns.

Key Terms
زوال ملك (zawāl al-milk): Cessation of ownership; here it is suspended (موقوف) meaning it is neither fully present nor fully absent.
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended; a legal status where the effect of a ruling is delayed pending a condition.
حربي (harbī): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Muslims; here applied analogically to a male apostate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on an apostate's lease of a house if he joins Dar al-Harb?
A) The lease continues.
B) The lease is invalidated.
C) The lease is suspended.
D) The lease becomes void from the start.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between apostasy affecting personal status (marriage, worship) which is immediate, and property, which is either suspended (Abu Hanifa) or fully retained (Sahibayn) until death or joining Dar al-Harb.

Apostate's Transactions: Suspension vs. Validity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on apostate's transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of an apostate's transactions and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The apostate's transactions are suspended according to Abu Hanifa but valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; the dispute hinges on legal capacity and ownership.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: what happens to an apostate's financial and contractual acts during his apostasy? The matn states the ruling of suspension, but the school's two other founding imams disagree. Understanding their proofs and the practical consequences is essential for applying the correct fatwa in cases of apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa (suspended), Abu Yusuf (valid as from healthy), and Muhammad (valid as from sick).
Note the different analogies used by the Sahibayn.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Explain why Abu Hanifa analogizes the apostate to a war captive (harbi) and how this leads to suspension of transactions.
Key concepts: qahr (subjugation), loss of isma (immunity), and the imam's choice.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Explain why Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold transactions valid: ahliyyah and milk exist; the apostate is addressed by the Law.
Notice the difference: Abu Yusuf considers likely return, Muhammad considers likely execution.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: if the apostate dies, transactions are void (Abu Hanifa) or valid and executed from his estate (Sahibayn).
Later preference (Abu al-Yusr) is for the Sahibayn's view.

قوله : ( وتوقف مبايعته وعتقه وهبته فإن آمن نفذ وإن هلك بطل) بيان لتصرفه حال ردته بعد بيان حكم أملاكه قبل ردته وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا هو جائز مطلقاً لأن الصحة تعتمد الأهلية وهي موجودة لكونه مخاطباً حكم أولاً بلحاقه الخ أن الحكم بعتق المدبر لا يكفي عن الحكم باللحاق بل لا بد من الحكم باللحاق قبل الحكم بعتق المدبر وهو خلاف ما في البحر اهـ. ثم رأيت في شرح المقدسي ما يؤيد ما في النهر حيث قال ما قاله المحقق في فتح القدير في غاية التحرير وفيه رد على ما في المجتبى، فالمراد أنه لا بد من وجود القضاء باللحاق لأنه شرط لتلك الأحكام بد من تحققه ليتحقق المشروط فإذا أراد القاضي الحكم بشيء من هذه الأحكام بدعوى ممن يتعلق به الحكم كالمدبر مثلاً فيقضي أولاً باللحاق ثم بالحكم المدعي لوجود تقدم الشرط على المشروط، وليس معناه ما يتوهم ظاهراً أنه يقضي أولاً باللحاق مستقلاً بلا دعوى حكم من وله نظير مذكور في محله .اهـ. قوله: (وقال محمد يعتبر وقت الحاقه) قدمنا عن شرح السير الكبير أن والشرط لا أحكامه صحيح بطريق الأولى لأن الرق يتصور معه ملك النكاح بخلاف قهر المرتد ؛ كذا في فتح القدير . أطلق المبايعة فشملت البيع والشراء والإجارة لأنها بيع المنافع، وأشار بالعتق إلى ما هو من حقوقه كالتدبير والكتابة فهما موقوفان أيضاً لكن لا يدخل الاستيلاد لأنه منه نافذ اتفاقاً لأنه لا يفتقر إلى حقيقة الملك حتى صح في جارية الابن. وأشار بالهبة إلى كل تمليك هو تبرع فدخلت الوصية فإنها موقوفة أيضاً، ولما كان الرهن من المعاوضات في المال كالبيع كان داخلاً فتوقف رهنه أيضاً، ولما كان قبض الدين مبادلة حكماً دخل تحت المبايعة فتوقف قبضه الدين أيضاً.
His statement: 'His sale, manumission, and gift are suspended; if he returns to Islam they are executed, and if he dies (as an apostate) they are void' is an explanation of his transactions during his apostasy after explaining the ruling on his properties before his apostasy. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said they are absolutely valid because validity depends on legal capacity (ahliyyah), which exists since he is addressed (mukhatab), and enforcement depends on ownership (milk), which exists because it stands before his death, except that according to Abu Yusuf they are valid as from a healthy person because he will likely return to Islam, and according to Muhammad they are valid as from a sick person because he will likely be killed. And for Abu Hanifa, he is a war captive subdued under our hands, as we established regarding the suspension of ownership and the suspension of transactions based on it, so he becomes like a harbi who enters our dar without aman and is captured; his transactions are suspended because his situation is suspended, since the imam has the choice between enslaving him or killing him. If he is killed or captured, these are not executed, or if he becomes Muslim, his property is not taken. Similarly here, there is a defect in legal capacity because he deserves to be killed; immunity (isma) is lost, unlike the adulterer and willful murderer because the death penalty is a penalty for the crime. Abu al-Yusr said: What they said is better because an apostate cannot be enslaved, and subjugation (qahr) is real, not legal; ownership is invalidated by legal subjugation, not real. For this reason, the ownership of one sentenced to stoning is not invalidated. The summary of the voidability is that the only thing that negates ownership is enslavement, but this is denied by Abu Hanifa; rather we say that enslavement became obligatory in principle due to the subjugation caused by his hostility, and this exists in the apostate, so it is established in him. That (the judgment of joining) must happen first, then the manumission of the mudabbar, as is known from their statements, so reflect. End. Abu al-Sa'ud said: The implication of his statement 'judged first concerning his joining, etc.' is that the judgment of manumission of the mudabbar is not sufficient without the judgment of joining; rather, the judgment of joining must precede the judgment of manumission, and this is contrary to what is in al-Bahr. End. Then I saw in Sharh al-Maqdisi what supports that in al-Nahr, where he said what the muhaqqiq said in Fath al-Qadir in Ghayat al-Tahrir, and in it is a refutation of what is in al-Mujtaba. The intended meaning is that the judgment of joining must exist because it is a condition for those rulings; it must be realized for the conditional to be realized. So if the judge wishes to rule on any of these rulings upon the claim of someone who is affected by the ruling, like the mudabbar for example, he first rules on the joining, then on the claimed ruling, because the condition precedes the conditional. It does not mean what is apparently misunderstood: that he first rules on the joining independently without a claim of a ruling; there is an example mentioned in its place. End. His statement: (And Muhammad considers the time of his joining) we have previously presented from Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir that if the condition is not fulfilled, its rulings do not apply. It is correct a fortiori because slavery can coexist with ownership of marriage, unlike the subjugation of the apostate; thus in Fath al-Qadir. He mentioned 'mubaaya'a' broadly, covering sale, purchase, and lease because it is a sale of benefits. He indicated by 'itq' (manumission) what is from its rights like tadbir and kitaba; these are also suspended, but istilad is not included because it is valid from him by agreement, as it does not require real ownership, so it is valid even for a son's slave-girl. He indicated by 'hibah' every gratuitous transfer, so wasiyyah is included; it is also suspended. Since rahn is a commutative contract regarding property like sale, it is included, so his rahn is also suspended. Since taking possession of a debt is an exchange legally, it falls under mubaaya'a, so his taking possession of a debt is also suspended.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ruing with proof) regarding whether the transactions of an apostate are suspended or valid. The matn states that according to Abu Hanifa, the apostate's contractual acts (sale, manumission, gift) are suspended (mawquf) during his apostasy: if he returns to Islam they are executed, if he dies as an apostate they are void. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold they are valid absolutely. Abu Yusuf considers him like a healthy person because of the likelihood of his return to Islam; Muhammad considers him like a sick person as he is likely to be killed. Abu Hanifa analogizes the apostate to a war captive (harbi) captured in dar al-Islam; his status is uncertain (the imam may kill or enslave him), so his transactions are suspended. The text further discusses when the judgment of joining dar al-harb is necessary, and then explains the scope of 'mubaaya'a', 'itq', and 'hibah' in the matn: for example, istilad (producing a child) is valid by consensus because it does not require full ownership. The passage also notes that rahn and taking possession of a debt are included under the suspension ruling.

Key Terms
مبايعته (mubaaya'atuhu): Includes sale, purchase, and lease (ijarah) as they are all forms of exchange.
استيلاد (istilad): Producing a child from a female slave; valid by consensus even for an apostate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the status of an apostate's sale and purchase during his apostasy?
A) Valid and enforceable
B) Absolutely void
C) Suspended pending return to Islam or death
D) Valid only if he returns to Islam

Key Takeaway
The apostate's transactions are disputed: Abu Hanifa suspends them; Sahibayn deem them valid. Later preference is for validity.

Liability for an Apostate's Torts: Ghasb and Itlaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee for apostate's torts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guarantee for ghasb/itlaf by an apostate and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn based on the method of proof.

Core Idea
An apostate's liability for property damage depends on how it is proven: mu'ayana vs iqrar.

This passage examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an apostate who usurps or destroys property. The central question is from which category of his earnings (kasb Islam or kasb ridda) the guarantee should be satisfied. The answer hinges on whether the act is proven by eyewitness testimony (mu'ayana) or by the apostate's own confession (iqrar), due to concerns about his credibility when heirs' rights are at stake.

Guided Path
1
State the baseline ruling
Identify that both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that an apostate who commits ghasb or itlaf must pay a guarantee (daman). The dispute is about which of his two earning categories the payment is taken from.
The two categories: kasb Islam (earnings as a Muslim) and kasb ridda (earnings as an apostate).
2
Map the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
According to Abu Hanifa, if proven by mu'ayana, the guarantee is taken first from kasb Islam, then from kasb ridda. According to the Sahibayn, it is taken from both equally. If proven by iqrar, Abu Hanifa takes it only from kasb ridda, while the Sahibayn still take from both.
The Sahibayn's position is more protective of the original owner's claim.
3
Understand the rationale behind the iqrar distinction
When the apostate confesses, he is suspected of lying to protect his Muslim earnings for the benefit of his heirs. Thus, Abu Hanifa limits the guarantee to kasb ridda, which would otherwise go to the state treasury (bayt al-mal).
This reflects the principle that the apostate's iqrar is not fully trustworthy when it harms the heirs.
4
Extend the ruling via qiyas to qatl khata
The author analogizes to unintentional killing (qatl khata) committed by an apostate. The same suspicion exists, so the same rule should apply: if proven by iqrar, the diyah is taken from kasb ridda only.
This extension shows the application of qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتح القدير : وعلى هذا لو غصب مالاً فأفسده يجب ضمانه في مال الإسلام وعندهما في الكل اهـ. وفي غاية البيان : إن حكم ما اغتصبه أو أتلفه كذلك عنده في كسب الإسلام فإن فضل شيء كان في كسب الردة. وفي التتارخانية : هذا إذا ثبت الغصب والإتلاف بالمعاينة، فإن ثبت بإقرار المرتد فعندهما يستوفى من الكسبين، وعنده من كسب الردة؛ كذا ذكر شيخ الإسلام اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون القتل خطأ كذلك لكونه متهما في إقراره لحق الورثة.
And in Fath al-Qadir: On this, if he usurps and spoils property, its guarantee is obligatory in the property of Islam, and according to the two (Sahibayn) in all (both). End. In Ghayat al-Bayan: The ruling of what he usurps or destroys is likewise according to him (Abu Hanifa) in the earnings of Islam; if something remains, it is in the earnings of apostasy. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: This is if the usurpation and destruction are established by eyewitness; if established by the confession of the apostate, then according to the two it is taken from both earnings, and according to him from the earnings of apostasy. Thus mentioned Shaykh al-Islam. End. And it befits that intentional killing be likewise because he is suspect in his confession regarding the right of the heirs.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling for an apostate who commits ghasb (usurpation) or itlaf (destruction) of property. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq cites Fath al-Qadir and other sources to explain the Hanafi positions. According to Abu Hanifa, if the apostate destroys property, the guarantee (daman) is taken from his kasb Islam (earnings as a Muslim) first, and then from kasb ridda. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is taken from both. There is a distinction based on how the liability is proven: if by eyewitnesses (mu'ayana), the guarantee is from both kasbs according to Sahibayn, but only from kasb Islam according to Abu Hanifa. If proven by the apostate's own confession (iqrar), then Sahibayn still take from both, but Abu Hanifa takes from kasb ridda only, because the apostate is suspected of lying to protect the heirs' share. The author notes that the same principle should apply to killing by mistake (qatl khata) because of the same suspicion. This shows the nuanced application of istihsan and qiyas in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation of property without right.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession of a party against himself.
معاينة (mu'ayana): Eyewitness testimony; direct observation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if an apostate's destruction of property is proven by his own confession, from which kasb is the guarantee taken?
A) Only from kasb Islam
B) Only from kasb ridda
C) From both equally
D) Not taken at all

Key Takeaway
The source from which an apostate's guarantee is satisfied depends on whether the liability is proven by eyewitnesses or his own confession, reflecting the Hanafi concern for evidentiary reliability and the protection of heirs' rights.
