Jinaya of Apostate Slaves and Mukatibs
Jinaya (tort) of apostate slaves and mukatibs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on jinaya involving apostate slaves and mukatibs, and distinguish between liability for torts committed by them versus torts committed upon them.

Core Idea
An apostate slave's torts are valid, but torts upon him are null.

This passage from Fath al-Qadir and al-Walwaljiyya clarifies the Hanafi position on tort liability when slaves or mukatibs apostatize. Understanding the distinction between torts committed by them and torts committed upon them is crucial, as it hinges on the continued validity of ownership and the unprotected status of the apostate's person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage addresses two distinct situations: (1) jinaya committed by an apostate slave/mukatib, and (2) jinaya committed upon an apostate slave.
2
Analyze the basis for each ruling
For the tortfeasor slave, ownership (milk) remains, so liability attaches to the master (or to the mukatib's earnings). For the victim slave, apostasy renders his person unprotected, so the tort is hader.
3
Note the special rule for mukatib
A mukatib owns his earnings during apostasy, so his tort liability falls on his earnings, not on the master.
This differs from a regular slave whose torts bind the master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتح القدير الولوالجية : وجناية العبد والأمة والمكاتب المرتدين كجنايتهم في غير الردة لأن الملك فيهما قائم بعد الردة والمكاتب يملك إكسابه في الردة فيكون موجب جنايته في كسبه والجناية على المماليك المرتدين هدر اهـ.
And in Fath al-Qadir and al-Walwaljiyya: The jinaya (injurious act) of the male slave, female slave, and mukatib (contract slave) who apostatize is like their jinaya in the absence of apostasy, because ownership over them remains after apostasy, and the mukatib owns his earnings during apostasy, so the consequence of his jinaya is in his earnings. And jinaya upon apostate slaves is invalid (hader). End.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This brief passage states the Hanafi ruling regarding jinaya (torts) involving slaves and mukatibs who have apostatized. The rule is that their jinaya is treated the same as if they had not apostatized. This is because ownership (milk) over the slave remains with the master even after the slave's apostasy; the master still owns the slave. For the mukatib (a slave under a contract of manumission), he owns his own earnings (kasb) even during apostasy, so any liability from his jinaya is borne by those earnings. Conversely, if someone commits a jinaya against an apostate slave, that act is considered hader (without liability) because the slave's person is not protected (ghayr ma'sum) due to apostasy. This is consistent with the general principle that apostasy renders the person of the apostate unprotected, but their property rights are preserved in specific ways.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a kitaba (contract of manumission) to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
هدر (hader): Legally null; without effect or liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, is jinaya upon an apostate slave (committed by a third party) subject to liability?
A) Yes, full diya
B) Yes, but reduced
C) No, it is hader
D) Only if the slave becomes Muslim again

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, apostasy of a slave does not affect his master's ownership or the mukatib's earnings; thus, torts committed by them are treated normally, but torts committed upon them are without liability.

Ruling on Selling Weapons to Rebels
Ruling on selling weapons to rebels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling weapons to rebels and explain the reasoning of i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya.

Core Idea
Selling weapons to rebels is makruh tahrimi because it assists in sin, but raw materials and items with permissible uses are not disliked.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the ruling on selling weapons to those engaged in rebellion. The principle is based on the concept of i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya (assistance in sin). However, the ruling is nuanced: it applies only to the weapon itself, not to raw materials, and only when the seller knows the buyer's intent. The passage also distinguishes between rebels and people of war, and extends the principle to other items like flutes and wine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being sold? Weapons (silah). To whom? Ahl al-fitna (rebels, highway robbers, thieves). What is the hukm? Makruh (disliked), specifically makruh tahrimi.
The passage explicitly states 'ukriha bai' al-silah min ahl al-fitna'.
2
Locate the reasoning
The reason given is 'li-annahu i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya' (because it is assistance in sin). This is the core principle.
Assistance in sin is a strong prohibition, hence the dislike is tahrimi.
3
Note the exception
If the seller does not know that the buyer belongs to ahl al-fitna, there is no dislike: 'wa in lam yadur annahu minhum la'.
The default presumption is that people are righteous (ghalabat al-amsar li-ahl al-salah).
4
Understand the restriction to weapons
The ruling applies only to the weapon itself (silah), not to raw materials like iron. Why? Because iron does not become a weapon except through craftsmanship (bi al-sina'a). The passage draws analogies: flutes vs. reeds, wine vs. grapes.
This shows the Hanafi principle: the ruling attaches to the object that is itself the instrument of sin.
5
Recognize the analogies and further distinctions
Rebels (ahl al-baghy) vs. people of war (ahl al-harb): selling iron to people of war is not permissible because they have time to craft weapons, but to rebels it is permissible because their rebellion is temporary. Also extend to other items: singing slave girl, butting ram, etc. are not disliked because their primary use is not sinful.
This illustrates case-by-case application of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره بيع السلاح من أهل الفتنة لأنه إعانة على المعصية وإن لم يدر أنه منهم لا. قيد بالسلاح لأن بيع ما يتخذ منه السلاح كالحديد ونحوه لا يكره لأنه لا يصير سلاحاً إلا بالصنعة، نظيره بيع المزامير يكره ولا يكره بيع ما يتخذ منه المزامير وهو القصب والخشب، وكذا بيع الخمر باطل ولا يبطل بيع ما يتخذ منه وهو العنب؛ كذا في البدائع. وذكر الشارح أن بيع الحديد لا يجوز من أهل الحرب ويجوز من أهل البغي والفرق أن أهل البغي لا يتفرغون لعمله سلاحاً لأن فسادهم على شرف الزوال بخلاف أهل الحرب .اهـ. وقد استفيد من كلامهم هنا أن ما قامت المعصية بعينه يكره بيعه وما لا فلا ولذا قال الشارح : إنه لا يكره بيع الجارية المغنية والكبش النطوح والديك المقاتل والحمامة الطيارة .اهـ. وذكر الشارح من الحظر والإباحة أنه لا يكره بيع جارية لمن لا يستبريها أو يأتيها من دبرها أو بيع غلام من لوطي اهـ. وفي الخانية من البيوع : ويكره بيع الأمرد من فاسق يعلم أنه يعصي به لأنه إعانة على المعصية اهـ. وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى في الحظر والإباحة تمامه أطلق في أهل الفتنة فشمل البغاة وقطاع الطريق واللصوص. قوله : (وإن لم يدر أنه منهم (لا) أي لا يكره البيع لأن الغلبة في الأمصار لأهل الصلاح وظاهر كلامهم في الأول أن الكراهة تحريمية لتعليلهم بالإعانة على المعصية والله أعلم بالصواب .
And it is disliked (makruh) to sell weapons to the people of sedition (ahl al-fitna) because it is assistance in sin. If he does not know that they are from them, then no [dislike]. It is restricted to weapons because selling what is used to make weapons, like iron and the like, is not disliked, for it does not become a weapon except through craftsmanship. An analogy is that selling flutes is disliked but selling what flutes are made from, like reeds and wood, is not disliked. Similarly, selling wine is invalid (batil) but selling what it is made from, like grapes, is not invalid, as stated in al-Bada'i. The commentator mentioned that selling iron is not permissible to the people of war but is permissible to the people of rebellion; the difference is that people of rebellion do not have time to fashion it into weapons because their corruption is on the verge of disappearing, unlike the people of war. End. It is understood from their statements here that whatever is itself the instrument of sin, selling it is disliked, and what is not, is not. Hence the commentator said: selling a singing slave girl, a butting ram, a fighting rooster, and a flying pigeon is not disliked. The commentator mentioned from the chapter on restriction and permissibility that it is not disliked to sell a slave girl to someone who will not observe her waiting period or will have intercourse with her from behind, or to sell a male slave to a pederast. End. In al-Khaniyya from the chapter on sales: It is disliked to sell a beardless boy to a sinful person who is known to sin with him, because it is assistance in sin. End. It will come, God willing, in the chapter on restriction and permissibility its completion. He generalized in 'people of sedition' to include rebels, highway robbers, and thieves. His statement: 'And if he does not know that they are from them, no' – meaning the sale is not disliked because the majority in cities are righteous people. And the apparent meaning of their statements in the first is that the dislike is prohibitive (tahrimiyya) because they reasoned it as assistance in sin. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement (hukm) regarding selling weapons to 'ahl al-fitna' (people of sedition, including rebels, highway robbers, and thieves). The core ruling is that it is makruh (disliked) to sell weapons to them because it assists in sin (i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya). However, if the seller does not know that the buyer belongs to them, there is no dislike. The ruling is specific to the weapon itself; raw materials like iron are not disliked to sell because they require craftsmanship to become weapons. The same principle applies to flutes vs. raw materials, and wine vs. grapes. The commentator distinguishes between selling iron to people of war (haram) versus people of rebellion (permissible) because rebels are preoccupied with their transient state and unlikely to craft weapons. The passage extends the principle to other items that are intrinsically instruments of sin: sale of them is disliked; items that can be used for sin but are not specifically made for it are not disliked. Examples given: singing slave girl, butting ram, fighting rooster, flying pigeon. Also, selling a slave girl to someone who will commit sin with her is not disliked because the sin is not inherent in the object. However, selling a beardless boy to a known sinner is disliked because the boy is directly the object of sin. The final note clarifies that if the seller is unaware of the buyer's identity, the sale is not disliked, as the presumption is that the majority of people are righteous. The apparent view is that the dislike is prohibitive (tahrimi) based on the reasoning of assisting sin.

Key Terms
أهل الفتنة (ahl al-fitna): People engaged in civil strife, including rebels (bughat), highway robbers (qutta' al-tariq), and thieves (lusus).
سلاح (silah): Weapons that are themselves instruments of warfare and aggression.
كراهة تحريمية (karaha tahrimiyya): Prohibitive dislike, close to haram, based on evidence that suggests prohibition but without certainty.
إعانة على المعصية (i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya): Assisting someone in committing a sin; the reason for the prohibitive dislike.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on selling iron to rebels (ahl al-baghy)?
A) It is makruh tahrimi like selling weapons.
B) It is haram.
C) It is permissible (ja'iz).
D) It is invalid (batil).

Key Takeaway
Selling weapons to rebels is makruh tahrimi because it assists in sin, but raw materials and items not inherently sinful are not disliked, and if the seller is unaware of the buyer's intent, there is no dislike.

Selling Weapons to People of Fitna: The Principle of Assistance in Sin
Commentary on the ruling that selling weapons to rebels is makruh due to assistance in sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that selling sin-inherent objects is makruh tahrimi and distinguish it from selling raw materials or items with incidental sin.

Core Idea
Selling weapons to rebels is makruh tahrimi because it is assistance in sin—but only the weapon itself, not its raw materials.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on selling weapons to people of fitna. The commentator clarifies that the dislike (karahah) is prohibitive because the act constitutes assistance in sin (i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya). He also provides a key distinction: only the object in which sin is inherent (like a weapon) carries this ruling; raw materials like iron or wood do not, because they require further manufacture to become instruments of sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling weapons to people of fitna (rebels, highway robbers, thieves). The ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
The commentator explicitly states the karahah is tahrimi because it is i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya.
2
Recognize the effective cause (illa)
The illa is that selling these objects is assistance in sin. This is the basis for the prohibitive dislike.
Quran 5:2 prohibits cooperation in sin and transgression.
3
Apply the analogy (qiyas) to other cases
The rule is analogized: selling flutes (sin-inherent instrument) is makruh, but selling reed/wood (raw material) is not. Similarly, selling wine is batil, but selling grapes is not. The key is whether the sin is inherent in the object itself.
The analogy shows that raw materials are not subject to the same ruling unless they are made into the sin-instrument.
4
Note the exception for unknown buyer status
If the seller does not know the buyer is from the people of fitna, the sale is not disliked. The default assumption is that the buyer is righteous.
This follows the principle that the majority in cities are people of righteousness.
5
Understand the tahrimi level
The dislike is prohibitive (tahrimi), meaning it is close to haram. This is because the reasoning (ta'lil) is i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya, which normally indicates prohibition.
The commentator explicitly says 'zahir anna al-karahah tahrimiyyah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره بيع السلاح من أهل الفتنة لأنه إعانة على المعصية وإن لم يدر أنه منهم لا. قيد بالسلاح لأن بيع ما يتخذ منه السلاح كالحديد ونحوه لا يكره لأنه لا يصير سلاحاً إلا بالصنعة، نظيره بيع المزامير يكره ولا يكره بيع ما يتخذ منه المزامير وهو القصب والخشب، وكذا بيع الخمر باطل ولا يبطل بيع ما يتخذ منه وهو العنب؛ كذا في البدائع. وذكر الشارح أن بيع الحديد لا يجوز من أهل الحرب ويجوز من أهل البغي والفرق أن أهل البغي لا يتفرغون لعمله سلاحاً لأن فسادهم على شرف الزوال بخلاف أهل الحرب .اهـ. وقد استفيد من كلامهم هنا أن ما قامت المعصية بعينه يكره بيعه وما لا فلا ولذا قال الشارح : إنه لا يكره بيع الجارية المغنية والكبش النطوح والديك المقاتل والحمامة الطيارة .اهـ. وذكر الشارح من الحظر والإباحة أنه لا يكره بيع جارية لمن لا يستبريها أو يأتيها من دبرها أو بيع غلام من لوطي اهـ. وفي الخانية من البيوع : ويكره بيع الأمرد من فاسق يعلم أنه يعصي به لأنه إعانة على المعصية اهـ. وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى في الحظر والإباحة تمامه أطلق في أهل الفتنة فشمل البغاة وقطاع الطريق واللصوص. قوله : (وإن لم يدر أنه منهم (لا) أي لا يكره البيع لأن الغلبة في الأمصار لأهل الصلاح وظاهر كلامهم في الأول أن الكراهة تحريمية لتعليلهم بالإعانة على المعصية والله أعلم بالصواب
And it is disliked (makruh) to sell weapons to the people of fitna because it is assistance in sin, and if one does not know that he is from them, then not. He restricted it to weapons because selling what is made from weapons like iron and the like is not disliked because it does not become a weapon except through manufacture. Its analogy: selling flutes is disliked, and selling what is made from flutes, which is reed and wood, is not disliked. Similarly, selling wine is invalid, and selling what is made from it, which is grapes, is not invalid; thus in al-Bada'i'. And the commentator mentioned that selling iron is not permissible to the people of war and is permissible to the people of rebellion (baghi), and the difference is that the people of rebellion do not have time to work it into weapons because their corruption is on the verge of removal, unlike the people of war. It is derived from their speech here that whatever sin is inherent in its essence, its sale is disliked, and whatever not. Hence the commentator said: there is no dislike in selling a singing slave-girl, the butting ram, the fighting rooster, and the flying pigeon. And the commentator mentioned from al-Hazr wa al-Ibahah that it is not disliked to sell a slave-girl to one who will not observe istibra' or will have anal intercourse with her, or selling a boy to a sodomite. And in al-Khaniyyah on sales: It is disliked to sell an amrad (beardless boy) to a corrupt person who it is known will commit sin with him because it is assistance in sin. And it will come, God willing, in al-Hazr wa al-Ibahah, its completion. He made it general in 'people of fitna' so it includes rebels, highway robbers, and thieves. His statement: (and if he does not know that he is from them, then no) meaning the sale is not disliked because the majority in the cities are people of righteousness. And it is apparent from their speech in the first that the dislike is tahrimi (prohibitive) because they reasoned it with assistance in sin. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling that selling weapons to people of fitna (rebels, highway robbers, thieves) is makruh. The author explains that only the weapon itself is subject to this dislike; selling raw materials like iron is not makruh because they only become weapons through manufacture. This is analogous to selling flutes (makruh) versus selling reed and wood (not makruh). Similarly, selling wine is batil, but selling grapes is not. The commentator further distinguishes between people of war (harbi) and people of rebellion (baghi): selling iron to a harbi is not permissible, but to a baghi it is permissible because their corruption is temporary. The general principle derived is that if the sin is inherent in the object itself (like weapons for fighting), selling it is disliked; if the sin is merely incidental (like a singing slave-girl used for music), there is no dislike. The passage also clarifies that if one does not know whether the buyer is from the people of fitna, the sale is not disliked because the presumption is that buyers are righteous. Finally, the commentator states that the dislike here is tahrimi (prohibitive) because the reasoning is 'assistance in sin'.

Key Terms
سلاح (silah): Weapons; the object itself is the focus of the ruling.
فتنة (fitna): Civil strife, rebellion; includes rebels, highway robbers, thieves.
إعانة على المعصية (i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya): Assistance in sin; the effective cause for prohibition or dislike.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because weapons are impure (najis).
B) Because it is assistance in sin (i'ana 'ala al-ma'siya).
C) Because it encourages rebellion.
D) Because it is an innovation (bid'a).

Key Takeaway
Selling sin-inherent objects to sinners is makruh tahrimi due to assistance in sin, but raw materials and items with incidental sin are exempt.

The Stranger Share: Disposal Rules in Partnership
States that each partner cannot dispose of the other's share without permission, with exceptions for original partnership and sale to partner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a partner may sell his share to a third party without the other partner's permission and explain the difference between original partnership and mixing.

Core Idea
In partnership, each partner is a stranger to the other's share and cannot dispose of it without permission.

Partnership is based on shared ownership, but this does not grant each partner agency over the other's share. This lesson clarifies the default rule and its exception: when a partnership arises by mixing separate properties, permission becomes necessary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Default Rule
Each partner is a stranger (ajnabi) regarding the other's share, so disposal to a third party requires permission. This is because partnership does not imply agency.
The matn phrase 'وكل أجنبي في قسط صاحبه' establishes this principle.
2
Identify the Exception for Sale to the Partner
Selling one's share to the partner is always permissible, as the partner can directly take possession of his own share.
This applies regardless of the type of partnership.
3
Distinguish Original Partnership from Mixing
In original joint ownership (e.g., buying or inheriting wheat together), each grain is jointly owned, so selling an undivided share to a third party is valid without permission. In mixing (khalat/ikhtilat), each particle is individually owned, and delivery to a third party would involve mixing with the other's share, thus requiring permission.
The key difference is the nature of ownership at the particle level.
4
Apply the Rule
Given a scenario of partnership, identify whether the partnership is original or by mixing, and determine if the proposed sale to a non-partner requires permission.
Remember: permission is only needed in mixing cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وكل أجنبي في قسط صاحبه أي وكل واحد من الشريكين ممنوع من التصرف في نصيب صاحبه لغير الشريك إلا بإذنه لعدم تضمنها الوكالة والقسط بالكسر الحصة والنصيب؛ كذا في القاموس. ولم يذكر المصنف حكم بيع أحدهما حصته وحكم الانتفاع بها أما الأول فقالوا : يجوز بيع أحدهما نصيبه من شريكه في جميع الصور ومن غير شريكه بغير إذنه إلا في صورة الخلط والاختلاط فإنه لا يجوز إلا بإذنه. والفرق أن الشركة إذا كانت بينهما من الابتداء بأن اشتريا حنطة أو ورثاها كانت كل حبة مشتركة بينهما فبيع كل منهما نصيبه شائعا جائز من الشريك والأجنبي بخلاف ما إذا كانت بالخلط أو الاختلاط كان كل حبة مملوكة بجميع أجزائها ليس للآخر فيها شركة، فإذا باع نصيبه من غير الشريك لا يقدر على تسليمه إلا مخلوطا بنصيب الشريك فيتوقف على إذنه بخلاف بيعه من الشريك
His saying: 'And each is a stranger regarding the share of his partner' means: each of the two partners is prohibited from disposing of the share of his partner for someone other than the partner except with his permission, because partnership does not include agency. Al-qist with kasra means portion and share; thus in al-Qamus. And the author did not mention the ruling on one of them selling his share nor the ruling on benefiting from it. As for the first, they said: It is permissible for one of them to sell his share to his partner in all forms, and to someone other than his partner without his permission except in the case of mixing (khalat) and intermingling (ikhtilat), for then it is not permissible except with his permission. The difference is that if the partnership is from the beginning by their buying wheat or inheriting it, every grain is shared between them; thus selling each of them his share as undivided is permissible from the partner and from the outsider. Contrast this with the case where it is by mixing or intermingling, where each grain is owned in its entirety, the other has no share in it; so if he sells his share to a non-partner, he cannot deliver it except mixed with the partner's share, so it depends on his permission, unlike his sale to the partner.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn phrase 'وكل أجنبي في قسط صاحبه' (and each is a stranger regarding the share of his partner). The commentator explains that the phrase means each partner is forbidden from disposing of the other partner's share to a third party without permission, because partnership does not automatically grant agency. The word 'قسط' (qist) means share or portion. The commentary then addresses a missing ruling: the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) did not explicitly state the ruling on selling one's share. The Hanafi scholars clarify: selling one's share to the partner is always permissible. Selling to a non-partner is generally permissible without the other partner's permission, except in cases where the partnership was formed by mixing (خلط) or intermingling (اختلاط) of separately owned properties. The reason for the difference is the nature of ownership: if the partnership was originally joint (e.g., buying wheat together), each grain is jointly owned, so selling an undivided share is straightforward. If the partnership arose by later mixing (e.g., each owned separate piles of wheat then mixed), each grain is individually owned; selling one's share to a non-partner would require delivering it mixed with the other's property, which needs permission. However, selling to the partner himself is always fine because the partner can take possession of his own share directly.

Key Terms
أجنبي (ajnabi): Stranger: a person who is not the partner; in this context, each partner is considered a stranger to the other's share regarding disposal to third parties.
قسط (qist): Share or portion; in this context, it refers to the specific share of a partner in the jointly owned property.
خلط (khalat): Mixing: the physical blending of separately owned properties, creating a situation where delivery is impossible without separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When partnership arises from inheritance
B) When partnership arises from mixing separate properties
C) When selling to the partner himself
D) When the property is liquid

Key Takeaway
A partner may sell his share to a third party without the other's permission except when the partnership arises from mixing separate properties, where permission is required.

Present Partner's Rights and Limitations in Joint Property
Rulings for a present partner in joint property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for a present partner's management of joint property with an absent co-owner, distinguishing between permissible cultivation/sale and prohibited leasing, and explain the istihsan exception.

Core Idea
A present partner may cultivate his share but must not lease the absent's share without permission—this is haram diyanatan though not prevented qada'an.

When a co-owner is absent, the present partner faces a dilemma: they need to manage the property but cannot act unilaterally over the absent partner's share. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rulings—what is permitted, what is prohibited, and how istihsan provides a pragmatic exception. The passage also introduces the critical distinction between religious obligation (diyanah) and court enforceability (qada').

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial recommendation
Note that the passage begins with a recommendation to raise the matter to the judge. This sets the default: judicial oversight is preferred.
The present partner is not compelled to go to court; if they do not, alternative rules apply.
2
Analyze permissible actions for the present partner
For land: the present partner may cultivate his share. For a vineyard: he may tend to it, sell the fruit, take his share, and sell the absent partner's share, keeping the proceeds. The absent partner upon return may ratify or demand compensation.
These actions are based on istihsan (juristic preference) as stated: 'This is istihsan and he adopted it.'
3
Examine the istihsan ruling on consumption of proceeds
Imam Muhammad reports that if the present partner takes his share of the sale price and consumes it, it is permissible. He sells the absent's share and preserves the price. If the absent never returns, the price is treated like a lost item (luqatah).
This is a departure from strict qiyas, allowing flexibility due to necessity.
4
Distinguish between diyanah and qada' regarding leasing
The present partner is forbidden (haram) from leasing or letting the absent partner's share without permission as a matter of religious duty (diyanatan). However, the judge does not prevent it (qada'an) because the absent partner has no representative to object.
This distinction is crucial: the act is sinful but not legally voidable by the court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثلاثين في التصرفات في الأعيان المشتركة فقال : أرض أو كرم بين حاضر وغائب أو بين بالغ ويتيم، فالحاضر أو البالغ يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي، ولو لم يرفع ففي الأرض يزرع بحصته ويطيب له ذلك ويقوم على الكرم فيبيع ثمره ويأخذ حصته ويوقف حصة الغائب ويبيع له ذلك، وإذا قدم الغائب ضمنه القيمة أو أجاز بيعه. وذكر في مواضع أخر عن محمد رحمه الله لو أخذ الشريك نصيبه من الثمن وأكله جاز ويبيع نصيب الغائب ويحفظ ثمنه، فلو حضر صاحبه يخير كما مر، فلو لم يحضر فهو كلقطة . قالت هذا استحسان وبه أخذ ولو أدى الخراج كان متبرعا. وذكر محمد رحمه الله صل : غاب أحد شريكي الدار فأراد الحاضر أن يسكنها رجلا أو يؤجرها لا ينبغي أن يفعل ذلك في ديانة إذ التصرف في ملك الغير حرام حقا لله تعالى وللمالك، ولا يمنع منه قضاء لأن الإنسان لا
And the thirtieth [section] on dispositions in jointly owned properties: he said: A land or a vineyard between a present and an absent person, or between an adult and an orphan; then the present or the adult raises the matter to the judge. And if he does not raise it, in the case of land he may cultivate his share, and that is permissible for him; and he tends to the vineyard, sells its fruits, takes his share, and stops the share of the absent one and sells it for him. And when the absent person returns, he either guarantees the price or ratifies the sale. And it is mentioned in other places from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): If the partner took his share of the price and consumed it, it is permissible; he sells the absent partner's share and preserves its price. Then if his partner arrives, he is given the choice as before; if he does not arrive, it is like a lost item (luqatah). He said: This is istihsan and he adopted it. And if he paid the kharaj (land tax), he is a volunteer. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: If one of the partners of a house is absent and the present partner wants to let someone live in it or rent it out, he should not do that religiously (diyanatan), because disposing of another's property is haram as a right of Allah and of the owner; and the judge does not prevent it because a person does not.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings for a present partner when the other partner is absent (or when one is an adult and the other a minor). It begins by advising that the matter should be taken to the judge; if not, the present partner may cultivate his share of the land or tend to a vineyard, sell the fruit, take his share, and keep the absent partner's share. When the absent partner returns, he may either accept the sale (paying the price) or demand compensation from the present partner. Imam Muhammad reports a position of istihsan (juristic preference): if the present partner takes his share of the sale proceeds and consumes it, it is permissible; he sells the absent partners share and keeps the payment safe. If the absent partner never returns, it becomes like a lost item (luqatah). However, the present partner is not entitled to recompense for taxes paid (kharaj); it is considered voluntary charity. Importantly, the present partner is not allowed to lease out the absent partner's share or let someone live in it without permission, as this would be using the property of another without consent, which is haram. The judge will not prevent it in court (because the absent partner has no representative), but it is sinful in the eyes of Allah.

Key Terms
غائب (gha'ib): Absent partner: one who is not present to manage the jointly owned property, requiring special rulings for the present partner.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: a method of reasoning that permits an exceptional ruling for ease or necessity, overriding strict analogy.
ديانة (diyanatan): Religiously/conscientiously: a ruling that binds the individual's conscience and accountability before Allah, even if not enforceable in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible with the intention of preservation
B) Not permissible because it is disposing of another's property without consent
C) Permissible only if the absent partner is expected to agree
D) Permissible if the rent is kept for the absent partner

Key Takeaway
A present partner may manage the property to preserve value but must not lease the absent partner's share without permission; the latter is haram diyanatan though not prevented qada'an.

Partnership Capital: Hanafi Conditions and Maliki Dispute
Establishes Hanafi ruling on partnership capital and disputes Maliki view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on partnership capital and compare it with the Maliki position, identifying the key principle of 'ribh ma lam yadman'.

Core Idea
Profit from unguaranteed assets invalidates partnership with commodities.

This lesson explores a classic inter-madhhab dispute: the Hanafi requirement that partnership capital must be currency (gold, silver, bullion, or coins) rather than commodities. Imam Malik permits commodities of the same genus, but the Hanafi school argues that such partnerships inevitably involve profiting from assets one has not guaranteed. Understanding this ruling is crucial for ensuring the validity of modern partnerships.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by articulating the ruling as given: Mufawada and 'Inan partnerships are only valid with the two currencies (naqdayn), bullion (tibr), and copper coins (fuloos).
The text first clarifies that owning a commodity does not break an existing partnership.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam Malik's school allows partnership with commodities, measured and weighed items, provided they are of the same genus.
Maliki reasoning: partnership is a contract on known capital, analogous to currency.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Maliki qiyas: partnership with commodities resembles partnership with currency because capital is known. Mudaraba is excluded as a textual exception.
The opponent's dalil is analogical (qiyas), not textual.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Study the Hanafi critique: partnership with commodities leads to 'ribh ma lam yadman' (profit from what is not guaranteed). When each sells their capital at different prices, one partner gains from the other's sale without having guaranteed that property.
The first transaction with commodities is sale (which cannot be joint), while with currency it is purchase (allows joint ownership).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: a Maliki can validly form a partnership with commodities; a Hanafi cannot. In fatwa, the Hanafi position is upheld.
The later Hanafi masters confirm this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشركة الشركة لا العرض ولا تصح مفاوضة وعنان بغير النقدين والتبر والفلوس ولو باع كل وإن فضل المال الذي لم يحصل به الشراء فسدت والفرق أنه في القدر إنما هو فضل أحدهما صاحبه فيما يصلح رأس مال المفاوضة فإن المشتري بينهما على الشركة ولأحدهما زيادة دراهم بخلاف الزيادة من حيث القيمة بعد الشراء فإنها حصلت في مال الغير لا في مال أحدهما فلم يفت التساوي في مالهما ؛ كذا في المحيط قوله : (لا العرض) أي لا تبطل بملك العرض لأنه لا تصح فيه الشركة فلا تشترط المساواة فيه، ولو قال لا ما لا تصح فيه لكان أولى ليدخل العقار والديون فإنها لا تبطل بهما إلا إذا قبض الديون قوله : (ولا تصح مفاوضة وعنان بغير النقدين والتبر والفلوس) وقال مالك : تجوز بالعروض والمكيل والموزون أيضاً إذا كان الجنس واحداً لأنها عقدت على رأس مال معلوم فأشبه النقود بخلاف المضاربة لأن القياس يأباها لما فيها من ربح ما لم يضمن فيقتصر على مورد الشرع. ولنا أنه يؤدي إلى ربح ما لم يضمن لأنه إذا باع كل واحد منهما رأس ماله وتفاضل الثمنان فما يستحقه أحدهما من الزيادة في مال صاحبه ربح ما لم يملك وما لم يضمن بخلاف الدراهم والدنانير لأن ثمن ما لا تتعين فكان يشتريه فى ذمته إذ هي . ربح ما ضمن ولأن أول التصرف في العرض البيع وفي النقود الشراء وبيع أحدهما ما له على أن يكون الآخر شريكاً في ثمنه لا يجوز، وشراء أحدهما شيئاً بماله على أن يكون المبيع بينه وبين غيره جائز
Book of Partnership. Partnership is not (invalidated by) the commodity, nor is Mufawada and 'Inan valid except with the two currencies (gold and silver), bullion, and copper coins. And if each sold... And if the wealth which was not acquired by purchase exceeds, it is invalid, and the difference is that in the quantity it is merely the excess of one over the other in what is suitable as capital of Mufawada; for the purchaser is between them on partnership, and one has an increase of dirhams, unlike the increase in value after purchase, for it has occurred in the wealth of another, not in the wealth of one of them, so equality in their wealth is not lost; thus in al-Muhit. His saying: (Not the commodity) meaning it is not invalidated by ownership of a commodity because partnership is not valid in it, so equality is not required in it. If he said 'not what is not valid in it', it would be better to include real estate and debts, for they are not invalidated by them except if the debts are collected. His saying: (And Mufawada and 'Inan are not valid except with the two currencies, bullion, and copper coins). Malik said: It is permitted with commodities, measured and weighed items too if the genus is one, because it is contracted on known capital, so it resembles currency, unlike Mudaraba because analogy rejects it due to containing profit of what is not guaranteed, so it is restricted to the text of the law. Our proof: It leads to profit of what is not guaranteed, because when each sells his capital and the two prices differ, what one deserves of the increase in the property of his companion is profit of what he did not own and what he did not guarantee, unlike dirhams and dinars because the price is not specified, so he buys it in his liability, it is profit of what he guaranteed. And because the first act in commodity is sale, and in currency is purchase. Sale by one of his property on condition that the other be a partner in its price is not permissible. Purchase by one of a thing with his property on condition that the sold item be between him and another is permissible.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of partnership (Mufawada and 'Inan) with non-currency items. The author states the Hanafi ruling: partnership is not invalidated by acquiring commodities (arood), but it is only valid with gold, silver, bullion, and copper coins. The commentary first clarifies that owning a commodity does not break the partnership, even if its value exceeds the other's capital, because equality is not required in commodity ownership. Then the main ruling: Mufawada and 'Inan are only valid with the two currencies (naqdain), bullion, and copper coins. Imam Malik's opposing view is presented: he allows partnership with commodities, as long as they are of the same genus, because it is a contract on known capital similar to currency. The Hanafi response is that such partnership leads to profiting from what is not guaranteed (ribh ma lam yadman). If both partners sell their capital and the prices differ, the one whose goods sell for more would profit from the other's property without having guaranteed it. This is unlike transactions with gold and silver, where the price is a debt in the partner's liability, so profit is earned on what is guaranteed. Additionally, the first transaction with commodities is sale, which makes the other a partner in price (invalid), while with currency it is purchase, which allows joint ownership of the purchased item (valid). Thus partnership with commodities is invalid due to this forbidden element.

Key Terms
عرض ('ard): Commodity or good that is not gold or silver currency. Plural: 'urood.
مفاوضة (mufawada): A type of partnership where partners are equal in capital, profit, and liability, and have mutual agency and guarantee.
عنان ('inan): A partnership where partners contribute capital but may differ in amounts and profit shares; does not require full mutual agency.
نقدين (naqdayn): The two currencies: gold (dinar) and silver (dirham).
تبر (tibr): Unrefined gold or silver, i.e., bullion.
فلوس (fuloos): Copper coins used as money (fuloos ra'ija).
ربح ما لم يضمن (ribh ma lam yadman): Profit earned from an asset one has not guaranteed (i.e., is not liable for its loss). This is a key principle prohibiting certain partnerships.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a partnership (Mufawada or 'Inan) with commodities ('urood) invalid?
A) Because commodities are impure and cannot be owned.
B) Because it leads to profiting from what is not guaranteed (ribh ma lam yadman).
C) Because the partners must have equal capital in weight.
D) Because commodities cannot be sold.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires partnership capital to be currency to avoid the prohibited element of profiting from unguaranteed assets.

Permissible Acts for Partners in 'Inan and Mufawada
Ruling statement with talil on partner's authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five acts permissible for a Hanafi partnership partner and explain why mudaraba is allowed despite being a form of partnership.

Core Idea
A partner may trade, hire, deposit, invest (mudaraba), and appoint agents—all for profit, because these are customary and profit-seeking.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains what actions partners in 'inan and mufawada partnerships may undertake without needing additional permission. It clarifies the scope of agency in Hanafi partnership law, emphasizing that profit is the ultimate goal. The text also addresses an internal disagreement regarding mudaraba, settling on the view that it is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Enumerated Acts
The text lists five actions: ibdaa (giving goods for trade without profit share), isti'jar (hiring), ida' (deposit), mudaraba (passive investment), and tawkil (appointing an agent). Familiarize yourself with each term.
All acts are customary (urf) or derived by qiyas.
2
Analyze the Justification for Each Act
Ibdaa and isti'jar are justified by custom. Ida' is allowed a fortiori (bi al-awla) because it is safekeeping without cost. Mudaraba is included because it is a lesser form of partnership and profit is the goal. Tawkil is implied by the partnership's agency.
The reasoning shows how Hanafi fiqh uses custom (urf) and analogy (qiyas).
3
Examine the Mudaraba Ikhtilaf
A narration from Abu Hanifa prohibits mudaraba because it resembles a new partnership. The preferred view (al-asahh) allows it, as adopted by al-Hidayah and al-Jawhara.
This intra-Hanafi dispute highlights tarjih (preferencing) methodology.
4
Distinguish Mudaraba from Musharaka
Unlike mudaraba, forming another partnership (musharaka) is not allowed without permission. The reason: mudaraba is a lesser contract that does not create a full partnership, whereas musharaka would duplicate the partnership relationship.
The principle: al-shay' la yastatbi' mithlahu (a thing does not include its like).
5
Apply the Ruling
In practice, a partner may undertake these five acts unless the partnership contract explicitly restricts them. The exception is musharaka, which requires the other partner's consent.
This rule applies to both 'inan and mufawada partnerships under Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكل من شريكي العنان والمفاوضة أن يبضع ويستأجر ويودع ويضارب ويوكل) بيان لما لكل منهما أن يفعله؛ أما البضاعة فلأنها معتادة في عقد الشركة. وفي القاموس الباضع الشريك والجمع بضع من بضع كمنع بضوعاً .اهـ. والمراد هنا دفع المال لآخر ليعمل فيه على أن يكون الربح لرب المال ولا شيء للعامل، وأما الاستئجار فلكونه معتاداً بين التجار وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا استأجر رجلاً ليتجر له أو ليحفظ المال، وأما الإيداع فجوازه بالأولى لأنه استحفاظ بغير أجر، وأما المضاربة فلكونها دون الشركة فتتضمنها. وعن أبي حنيفة ليس له ذلك لأنه نوع شركة والأول أصح وهو رواية الأصل لأن الشركة غير مقصودة وإنما المقصود تحصيل الربح كما إذا استأجره بأجر بل أولى لأنه تحصيل بدون ضمان ذمته بخلاف الشركة حيث لا يملكها لأن الشيء لا يستتبع مثله ؛ كذا في الهداية. وبهذا علم أنه ليس للشريك أن يشارك بخلاف المضاربة ولذا قال ويضارب» ولم يقل ويشارك». قال في الجوهرة إلا بإذن شريكه.
And each of the partners in 'inan and mufawada may give merchandise (ibdaa), hire (isti'jar), deposit (ida'), enter into mudaraba, and appoint an agent (tawkil). This is an explanation of what each may do. As for ibdaa, it is customary in a partnership contract. In al-Qamus: al-badi' is the partner, plural bida' from bada'a yabda'u budoo'an. The intended meaning here is giving money to another to work with it on the condition that the profit belongs to the owner of the money and there is nothing for the worker. As for hiring, it is customary among merchants, and it was left unqualified so it includes hiring a man to trade for him or to guard the money. As for deposit, its permissibility is a fortiori because it is safekeeping without compensation. As for mudaraba, it is because it is lesser than partnership and is included in it. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he does not have that (i.e., mudaraba) because it is a type of partnership, but the first is more correct and it is the narration of the original because partnership is not intended; rather, the intended goal is to obtain profit, just as if he hired him for wages, even more so because it is obtaining without guaranteeing his liability, unlike partnership where he does not possess it because a thing does not include its like; thus in al-Hidayah. By this it is known that a partner may not enter into another partnership, unlike mudaraba, and therefore he said 'yudarrib' and did not say 'yusharik'. It is stated in al-Jawhara: except with the permission of his partner.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It explains the permissible acts for partners in 'inan and mufawada partnerships. The author enumerates five actions: ibdaa (giving merchandise for trade without profit share for the worker), isti'jar (hiring), ida' (deposit), mudaraba (passive partnership), and tawkil (appointing an agent). Each is justified by custom or legal reasoning. Notably, mudaraba is allowed even though it resembles a new partnership because the goal of partnership is profit, and mudaraba achieves that without the liability of a full partnership. The author clarifies that forming another partnership (musharaka) is not allowed without permission, as stated in al-Jawhara.

Key Terms
يبضع (yabda'u): To give merchandise or money to another to trade with, with profit belonging to the owner and no wage for the worker.
شريك العنان (sharīk al-'inān): A partner in a limited partnership where each partner contributes capital and has equal or proportional ownership but no agency beyond the partnership.
المفاوضة (al-mufāwaḍa): A general partnership where partners have full agency and equal shares in profits and losses.
المضاربة (al-muḍāraba): A silent partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, sharing profits according to agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Giving goods on ibdaa
B) Hiring an employee for the partnership business
C) Entering into a new partnership (musharaka) with a third party
D) Appointing an agent to trade

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi partnership law, partners may engage in customary profit-seeking transactions, and while mudaraba is allowed, forming a new partnership (musharaka) requires consent.

Istihsan Overriding Qiyas in Partnership Loans ('Ariyah)
Discussion of 'ariyah (loan) by a partner: analogy and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a partner lending partnership property and distinguish between the qiyas and istihsan positions.

Core Idea
A partner may lend partnership property by istihsan, even though qiyas would forbid it.

This passage from al-Kafi presents a classic example of istihsan overriding qiyas in Hanafi fiqh. When a partner lends a partnership asset without compensation, strict analogy would prohibit it and impose liability. However, juristic preference (istihsan) permits it, reflecting customary mercantile practice and avoiding hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under discussion? State the ruling by qiyas and by istihsan.
The act is a partner lending a partnership asset (e.g., an animal) without compensation.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Why does qiyas prohibit the loan and make the lending partner liable for half the value?
Qiyas treats the loan as an unauthorized disposition of joint property, so the lender is liable to the other partner.
3
Recognize the istihsan override
Why does al-Hakim apply istihsan to remove liability?
Istihsan considers custom and avoidance of hardship; merchants commonly lend partnership items without expecting liability.
4
Confirm the consensus
Which Hanafi authorities hold this position?
The ruling is attributed to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
5
Apply to similar cases
What other items are analogized?
The same ruling applies to lending a garment, house, or servant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنها العارية. قال الحاكم في الكافي وليس له أن يعير في القياس فإن فعل؛ فإن أعار دابة فعطبت تحت المستعير فالقياس فيه أن المعير ضامن لنصف قيمة الدابة لشريكه ولكني أستحسن أن لا أضمنه، وهذا قياس قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ومحمد، وكذلك لو أعار ثوباً أو داراً أو خادماً اهـ.
Among them is 'ariyah (loan). Al-Hakim said in al-Kafi: He does not have the right to lend by analogy (qiyas). If he does lend, and he lends an animal and it perishes in the hands of the borrower, then by qiyas the lender is liable for half the value of the animal to his partner, but I (al-Hakim) apply istihsan and do not make him liable. And this is the position (qawl) of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. Similarly, if he lends a garment, a house, or a servant.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, discussing the permissibility of a partner lending partnership property ('ariyah). The author presents the position from al-Kafi: by strict analogy (qiyas), a partner cannot lend partnership property because it is a gratuitous contract not customary in trade. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), the lending is valid and if the item perishes, the lending partner is not liable to the partner. This ruling is attributed to the three major Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, indicating it is the established position of the school.

Key Terms
العارية (al-'āriyah): A loan of a tangible item for use without compensation, not intended for profit-making.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The lending partner must pay full compensation to the other partner
B) The lending partner must pay half the value to the other partner
C) The lending partner is not liable to the other partner
D) The borrower must pay the full value to both partners

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a partner to lend partnership property without liability when done by custom, applying istihsan over strict qiyas.

One Partner Deferring a Partnership Debt – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ta'jil by one partner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the validity of one partner deferring a partnership debt, and explain the underlying analogy to agency.

Core Idea
The authority to defer a debt depends on who contracted and which imam's view is followed.

This passage from al-Muhit presents a classical intra-Hanafi dispute on a practical partnership issue: can one partner unilaterally extend the payment term of a debt owed to the partnership? The answer differs based on whether the partner was the original contracting party, and even on which of the great imams you follow. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for modern partnership law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Scenarios
List the three cases mentioned: contracting partner defers, non-contracting partner defers, and both contract and one defers. Note which imam's view is stated for each.
The text breaks it into three 'awjuh' (aspects).
2
State the Positions of Each Imam
For each scenario, record the ruling according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Pay attention to when 'the two' (Sahibayn) agree and when they diverge.
Abu Yusuf has a distinct individual opinion separate from Muhammad in one scenario.
3
Understand the Analogy to Agency
The text compares the contracting partner's authority to that of an agent (wakil) who sold goods. An agent can modify payment terms; similarly, the contracting partner has that authority. Identify the specific analogy and its limits.
The text says: 'the principle is: the agent for sale, if he grants a remission...'
4
Trace the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Determine the practical consequence of each view. For example, if a non-contracting partner defers, under Abu Hanifa the whole deferment is void; under Sahibayn it is valid for his own share only. This affects the other partner's right to demand immediate payment.
This difference directly impacts real transactions.
5
Evaluate the Application to Modern Partnerships
Consider how this ruling applies to modern commercial partnerships where one partner manages daily operations. Which view would be most practical? Why might jurists prefer the Sahibayn's position?
No explicit tarjih in the text; this is an open analytical step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها تأجيل أحدهما الدين. قال في المحيط : وإن كان لهما دين على آخر فأجله أحدهما فهو على ثلاثة أوجه : إن أجله العاقد جاز في النصيبين ولا يضمن نصيب شريكه عندهما وعند أبي يوسف يجوز في نصيبه ولا يجوز في نصيب شريكه. وأصله الوكيل بالبيع إذا أبرأ عن الثمن أو حط أو أجله عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف إلا أن هناك يضمن من ماله لموكله عندهما وهنا لا يضمن لأن العاقد هنا لو أقال العقد ثم باعه بنفسه جاز، فلما ملك إنشاء البيع بثمن إلى أجل فلأن يملك التأجيل فيه أولى. ولو أجل غير العاقد أو عقدا جميعاً فأجله أحدهما لم يجز عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يجوز في نصيبه.
Among them is the deferment (ta'jil) of the debt by one partner. It is stated in al-Muhit: If they have a debt owed by someone, and one of them defers it, it is of three aspects: If the one who conducted the original contract defers it, it is valid for both shares and he does not guarantee his partner's share according to the two (Abu Yusuf? Actually 'indahuma means Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And according to Abu Yusuf (singular), it is valid for his own share but not for his partner's share. The principle is: the agent for sale, if he grants a remission of the price or reduces it or defers it, according to the two it is valid contrary to Abu Yusuf, except that there he guarantees from his own wealth for his principal, whereas here he does not guarantee because here if the contracting party rescinded the contract and then sold it himself, it would be valid. Since he is able to initiate a sale for a deferred price, he is more entitled to defer. And if the one who did not conduct the contract defers, or if both conducted the contract and one of them defers, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two it is valid in his own share.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of one partner unilaterally deferring a debt owed to the partnership. The text from al-Muhit outlines three scenarios. The key distinction is whether the partner deferring was the original contracting party (al-'aqid). If yes, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Sahibayn), the deferment is valid for both shares without liability. But according to Abu Yusuf's individual opinion, it is only valid for his own share. If the deferring partner was not the contracting party, Abu Hanifa says it is invalid entirely, while the Sahibayn say it is valid for his own share only. The reasoning ties into agency principles: an agent who conducts a sale has authority to modify the terms, but a non-agent partner does not. The passage provides a detailed legal comparison.

Key Terms
التأجيل (at-ta'jīl): Deferring the payment date of a debt to a later time.
العاقد (al-'āqid): The person who conducted the original contract (sale or loan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The deferment is valid for both shares
B) The deferment is valid only for his own share
C) The deferment is invalid entirely
D) The deferment is valid only if the other partner consents

Key Takeaway
The validity of a partner deferring a partnership debt hinges on whether he was the original contractor and which Hanafi imam's opinion is applied.

Distinctive Rulings for Mufawada vs 'Inan Partners
List of acts permissible for mufawada partners but not for 'inan partners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts are permissible for a mufawada partner but not for an 'inan partner, and apply the rulings to scenarios involving gifts, agency, and third-party partnerships.

Core Idea
Mufawada partners have broader agency—they can contract kitaba, permit trade, and marry slaves—'inan partners cannot.

In Hanafi partnership law, mufawada and 'inan partnerships differ in the scope of each partner's authority. This passage from al-Muhit enumerates specific acts that a mufawada partner may perform independently, but an 'inan partner may not. It also clarifies related rules on gifts, agency, and the ability to form new partnerships. Master these distinctions to correctly identify the limits of partner authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two partnership types
Recall the definitions: mufawada is a general partnership with full agency and mutual guarantee; 'inan is a limited partnership with restricted authority.
Mufawada requires equal capital, profit, and liability.
2
List the acts exclusive to mufawada
From the text, extract: kitaba (contract of manumission by payment), granting permission to a slave to trade, and marrying a female slave from partnership property.
These are not permitted for 'inan partners.
3
Note acts prohibited for both
Identify that marrying a male slave and freeing a slave on partnership wealth are disallowed for both types.
These require the partner's share to be unaffected.
4
Understand gift and agency rules
Accepting consumables (fruits, bread) from a mufawada partner is allowed without liability; giving valuable items is not valid in the partner's share. If a mufawada partner appoints an agent, the other partner can forbid; if not forbidden, the agent's purchase binds both.
Istihsan is the basis for no liability on the receiver.
5
Apply third-party partnership rules
A mufawada partner may enter an 'inan partnership with a third party without permission; a mufawada partnership requires permission, else defaults to 'inan.
This qualifies the earlier absolute statement that a partner cannot enter another partnership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم يجوز للمفاوض ما لا يجوز لشريك العنان فيجوز له كتابة العبد والإذن بالتجارة وتزويج الأمة دون شريك العنان، ولا يجوز للكل تزويج العبد ولا الإعتاق على المال، وقبول هدية المفاوض وأكل طعامه والاستعارة منه بغير إذن شريكه جائز ولا ضمان على الأكل والمتصدق عليه استحساناً. ولو كسا ثوباً أو وهبه لم يجز في حصة شريكه وإنما يجوز في الفاكهة والخبز واللحم وأشباهه. ولو وكل المفاوض رجلاً بشراء شيء فنهاه الآخر صح نهيه وإن لم ينهه حتى اشترى يرجع بالثمن على أيهما شاء، ولغير المشتري أن يرد المبيع بالعيب. ولو شارك أحدهما آخر عناناً جاز عليهما لأن شركة العنان أخص وأدون من المفاوضة، وإن شارك مفاوضة جاز بإذن شريكه وبدون إذنه تنعقد عناناً؛ كذا في المحيط. وبه تبين أن قولهم كما كتبناه أولاً أن الشريك ليس له أن يشارك ليس على إطلاقه
Then know: permitted for the mufawada partner what is not permitted for the 'inan partner. Thus he may write a contract of kitaba for a slave, grant permission for trade, and marry a female slave, but not the 'inan partner. And, for all (both), it is not permitted to marry a male slave or to free on the partnership's wealth. Accepting a gift from a mufawada partner, eating his food, and borrowing from him without his partner's permission are permissible, and there is no liability on the eater or the recipient of charity by istihsan. If he clothes someone with a garment or gives it as a gift, it is not valid in his partner's share, but it is valid in fruits, bread, meat, and similar things. And if the mufawada partner appoints a man to buy something and the other partner forbids him, his forbiddance is effective; if he does not forbid and he buys, he may recover the price from whichever partner he wishes, and the non-buyer has the right to return the sold item due to defect. And if one of them enters into an 'inan partnership with another, it is valid upon both (the original partners) because 'inan partnership is more specific and lower than mufawada. And if he enters into a mufawada partnership, it is valid with his partner's permission; without permission, it becomes an 'inan partnership. Thus in al-Muhit. And by this it is clear that their statement, as we wrote earlier, that a partner may not enter into a partnership is not absolute.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage summarizes the special privileges of a mufawada partner compared to an 'inan partner. It first lists acts that a mufawada partner may do alone: kitaba (contract of manumission by payment), giving permission to a slave to trade, and marrying a female slave. However, even mufawada partners cannot marry a male slave or emancipate a slave using partnership property. Then it discusses gifts and loans: accepting small consumables (fruits, bread) is allowed without liability, but giving away a garment or valuable item is not valid for the partner's share. Then it deals with agency: if a mufawada partner appoints an agent and the other partner forbids, the forbiddance is effective; but if the agent buys before being forbidden, the seller can claim the price from either partner. Then it addresses a partner's ability to start a new partnership: an 'inan partnership with a third party is valid even without permission; a mufawada partnership requires permission, otherwise it defaults to 'inan. This last point resolves an earlier statement that 'a partner may not enter into a partnership' — it is not absolute.

Key Terms
المفاوضة (al-mufāwaḍa): A general partnership where each partner has full agency and responsibility for all partnership activities.
العنان (al-'inān): A limited partnership where each partner contributes capital and shares profit proportionally, but agency is restricted to the partnership's scope.
كتابة (kitāba): A contract of manumission where the slave pays his master in installments for freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions is permissible for a mufawada partner without his partner's permission, but NOT for an 'inan partner?
A) Freeing a slave from partnership property
B) Marrying a female slave from partnership property
C) Accepting a gift of bread from a third party
D) Appointing an agent to buy goods for the partnership

Key Takeaway
Mufawada partners have exclusive authority to perform certain acts (kitaba, permission to trade, marriage of female slaves) that 'inan partners cannot do alone, but both are restricted from acts that would waste partnership property.

Rulings on Purchase, Loan, and Acknowledgment in 'Inan Partnership
States three distinct rulings on transactions in an 'inan partnership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three key rulings on transactions within an 'inan partnership and apply them to determine liability distribution.

Core Idea
In an 'inan partnership, a loan binds both partners, but an acknowledgment of debt binds only the acknowledging partner.

This source passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq outlines three practical scenarios in an 'inan partnership. Understanding these rules is essential for anyone engaging in partnership commerce. The text clarifies the limits of mutual agency: certain transactions like loans are binding on the partnership, while others like acknowledgments are personal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Extract from the passage: (1) purchase when one partner enters a new partnership with a third, (2) loan taken by one partner, and (3) acknowledgment of a debt by one partner.
2
Analyze the purchase scenario
When one partner forms a new 'inan partnership with a third, purchases by the third are split: half to the third, half shared equally between the two original partners. Purchases by the non-participating original partner are shared only with his original partner, excluding the third.
The new partnership is separate from the original.
3
Analyze the loan scenario
A loan taken for trade by one partner obligates both partners. The reason given: a loan is an exchange of property (tamlik mal bi mal), analogous to currency exchange (sarf), which requires immediate exchange and creates joint liability.
Joint liability arises because the loan is a transaction, not a personal act.
4
Analyze the acknowledgment scenario
If one partner acknowledges that a third party borrowed from the partnership, only that partner is liable. The acknowledgment is a personal statement and does not bind the other partner, as it is not a transaction.
Acknowledgment is not an exchange of property.
5
Synthesize the principle
The key distinction is whether the action involves an exchange of property (tamlik): if yes, it binds the partnership; if no, it binds only the acting partner.
This principle limits mutual agency to trade transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضمن حصة الشريك، ولو شارك أحدهما رجلاً شركة عنان فما اشترى الشريك الثالث كان النصف للمشتري والنصف بين الشريكين الأولين وما اشتراه الشريك الذي لم يشارك فهو بينه وبين شريكه نصفين ولا شيء منه للشريك الثالث ولو استقرض أحد شريكي العنان مالاً للتجارة لزمهما لأنه تمليك مال بمال فكان بمنزلة الصرف ولو أقر أحد الشريكين أنه فلان ألفاً استقرض من من تجارتهما تلزمه خاصة .اهـ.
Within the share of the partner. And if one of the two [partners] partnered a man with an 'inan partnership, then whatever the third partner bought: half is for the buyer and half is between the first two partners; and whatever the partner who did not participate [in that purchase] bought, it is between him and his partner in halves, and nothing of it is for the third partner. And if one of the two 'inan partners borrowed money for trade, it obligates both, because it is an exchange of property for property, thus it is like currency exchange. And if one of the two partners acknowledged that Zaid borrowed a thousand from their business, it only binds him specifically. End.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct rulings concerning 'inan partnership. First, regarding joint purchase: when one partner joins a third party in a new 'inan partnership, any purchase made by that third partner is divided such that half belongs to the third partner (the buyer) and the other half is shared equally between the two original partners. Conversely, if the partner who did not join the new partnership makes a purchase, it is shared equally between him and his original partner, and the third partner gets nothing. Second, if one partner takes a loan for trade purposes, both partners are liable because a loan is an exchange of property for property, analogous to currency exchange (sarf), which requires immediate exchange and creates joint liability. Third, if one partner acknowledges that someone borrowed money from the partnership, only the acknowledging partner is liable, not the other partner, because the acknowledgment pertains to that partner's own authority. These rulings clarify the limits of mutual agency in 'inan partnership: transactions that involve exchange of property (like loans) bind the partnership, while mere acknowledgments do not.

Key Terms
شركة عنان (sharikat al-‘inān): A partnership where each partner contributes capital and labor, with mutual agency but not mutual guarantee (as opposed to mufawada).
تمليك مال بمال (tamlik māl bi māl): An exchange of property for property, like a sale or loan, which immediately transfers ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the borrowing partner
B) Both partners jointly
C) Neither partner
D) Only the non-borrowing partner if he agreed

Key Takeaway
In an 'inan partnership, loans for trade bind both partners, but acknowledgments of debt bind only the acknowledging partner.

Mufawada Partner Waiving Sale Price: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on waiver of price in mufawada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on a partner waiving the sale price in a mufawada partnership, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In mufawada, the seller's waiver of the price is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but not according to Abu Yusuf.

This passage from al-Zahiriyyah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a partner in a mufawada partnership can unilaterally waive (by gift or ibra') the sale price after selling an item from the partnership trade. The dispute highlights the tension between the partner's personal authority over his share and the joint nature of partnership assets. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for determining the buyer's liability and the partnership's claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
A mufawada partner sells an item from the partnership trade. After the sale, the seller gifts the price to the buyer or discharges him (ibra'). Is this valid?
The sale itself is valid; the dispute concerns only the waiver of the price.
2
State the positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the gift or ibra' is permissible (jāz). Abu Yusuf holds that it is not valid.
All three are Hanafi imams; this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Identify the consensus point
If someone other than the seller (e.g., the other partner or a third party) gifts the price, it is valid only in that person's share by consensus.
This shows that the disagreement is specific to the seller's authority over the full price.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the buyer is freed from the price obligation entirely. Under Abu Yusuf, the buyer remains liable for the full price to the partnership.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the real-world effect on the buyer and the partnership's assets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : إذا باع أحد المتفاوضين شيئاً من تجارتهما ثم إن البائع وهب الثمن من المشتري أو أبرأه منه جاز في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد خلافاً لأبي يوسف، ولو وهب غير البائع جاز في حصته فقط إجماعاً.
In al-Zahiriyyah: If one of the two partners in a mufawada partnership sold something from their trade, then the seller gifted the price to the buyer or discharged him from it, it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And if someone other than the seller gifted it, it is permissible only in his share by consensus.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage highlights an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a mufawada partnership. The issue: after a partner sells an item from the partnership's merchandise, he then waives the price by either gifting it to the buyer or giving a receipt (ibra'). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that such a gift or discharge is valid and effective, meaning the buyer is freed from the price obligation. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the price becomes part of the partnership assets and cannot be waived unilaterally. However, if a third party (not the seller) gifts the price, all agree that it is valid only in that third party's own share. The ruling applies specifically to mufawada partnership, which involves full mutual agency and guarantee. The dispute revolves around whether the seller retains authority over the price after the sale or whether it becomes a joint asset.

Key Terms
المفاوضة (al-mufāwaḍah): A partnership where partners have full mutual agency and guarantee, including personal liability.
أبرأه (abra'ahu): To discharge someone from a debt or obligation, i.e., to grant a receipt (ibra').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mufawada partnership, if the seller (one partner) gifts the sale price to the buyer, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) It is invalid because the price belongs to both partners
B) It is valid and the buyer is discharged
C) It is valid only if both partners consent
D) It is null and void

Key Takeaway
In mufawada, a partner's unilateral waiver of the sale price is valid for his share according to the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad); Abu Yusuf holds the price remains joint property.

The Partner's Hand is a Trust (Amanah)
Explains why the partner's hand over partnership property is considered a trust, not a guarantee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal classification of a partner's possession of partnership property as amanah and explain why it differs from possession as a guarantee.

Core Idea
A partner's possession is a trust, not a guarantee, because it is taken with permission and not as compensation.

Partnership involves shared property, but what is the legal nature of each partner's control over that property? This passage clarifies that the partner is a trustee (amanah holder), not a guarantor. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining liability in case of loss or claims of return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Note the explicit statement: the partner's hand over partnership property is amanah (trust). This is the central ruling.
The ruling is from the matn and confirmed by the sharh.
2
Analyze the Reasoning (Talil)
The reason given: the partner took possession with the owner's permission, not as a substitute (like price) or as a pledge. These two exclusions define the boundary.
The exclusions (sawm al-shira and rahn) are examples of non-amanah possession.
3
Compare with Other Categories
Contrast with property taken on approval (guarantee) and pledge (guarantee). This clarifies the essential condition for amanah.
The text explicitly excludes these by 'the first' and 'the second'.
4
Apply the Subsidiary Ruling
If a partner claims to have returned the property to his co-partner, his statement is accepted with an oath. This follows from the trust status.
This ruling applies both during life and after death, showing its breadth.
5
Note the Authority
The reasoning is from al-Hidayah; the exclusions from al-Nihayah; the subsidiary ruling is supported by al-Walwaliji in agency.
This shows the layered doctrinal support (matn, sharh, other works).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويده في المال (أمانة أي الشريك لأنه قبض المال بإذن المالك لا على وجه البدل والوثيقة فصار كالوديعة ؛ كذا في الهداية. وخرج بالأول المقبوض على سوم الشراء، وبالثاني الرهن كما في النهاية : وظاهر كلامهم هنا أنه لو ادعى دفع المال إلى شريكه فالقول له مع اليمين، سواء كان في حياته أو بعد موته وظاهر كلام الولوالجي في الوكالة يفيده فإنه قال :
His statement: (And his hand over the property is a trust) i.e., the partner, because he took possession of the property with the owner's permission, not as a compensation or pledge, so he becomes like a depositary; thus in al-Hidayah. And by the first the property taken upon contemplation of purchase is excluded, and by the second the pledge as in al-Nihayah. And the apparent meaning of their speech here is that if he claims to have handed the property to his partner, his statement is accepted with an oath, whether during his lifetime or after his death. And the apparent speech of al-Walwaliji in the chapter of agency indicates it, for he said:
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage defines the legal nature of a partner's possession of partnership property. The author clarifies the statement from the matn that 'his hand over the property is a trust' (amana). The reason is that the partner took the property with the permission of the owner (the other partner), and not as a substitute for something (like a price in a sale) or as a pledge (which would create liability). Therefore, the partner is analogous to a depositary (wadi') – his possession is custodial, not guaranteeing. If the property is lost without negligence, the partner is not liable. This is unlike property taken on approval to purchase (which is a guarantee) or a pledge (which is also a guarantee). The text also notes that if a partner claims he returned the property to his partner, his statement is accepted with an oath, both in life and after death. This is supported by a statement of al-Walwaliji in the chapter on agency, which is referenced but not fully quoted here.

Key Terms
أمانة (amānah): Property held in trust, with no liability for loss unless due to negligence or transgression.
الوديعة (al-wadī‘ah): A deposit: property given to someone for safekeeping without compensation.
سوم الشراء (sawm al-shirā’): Property taken on approval to purchase; possession is a guarantee, not a trust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he owns part of it
B) Because he took it with permission and not as a substitute or pledge
C) Because he is a co-owner
D) Because it is a loan

Key Takeaway
The partner's possession is a trust (amanah) because it is taken with permission and not as a substitute; thus, liability only arises from transgression or negligence.
